  Gebhardt (PSE). – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, the arrogance of many men – and I would like to make it clear that I mean many, and not all – is quite unmistakable, even in this House. To say that equality is possible if we simply make everything absolutely equal in legal terms is really so arrogant that one can hardly comprehend it. All the things we are discussing here – curriculum vitae, employment structure, the world of work, the conditions governing appointment and promotion – all these things are based on the lives and experiences of men. And they say to us, in all seriousness, ’Yes ladies, all you have to do is to become like us men, and then you too will have equal opportunities’. Do they seriously believe that that is what we want, that we do not want to carry on being ourselves? Surely that is not true! Really that cannot be true!

The family is a valuable thing, it is true, but it is not everything. We should not forget that the family is not, in fact, the norm, or the universally applicable way of life in our society. It is becoming less and less so. And a family does not mean having a marriage certificate. Family means people with children, and what does a marriage certificate have to do with that? It is a question of living together, of being together, and that is what has to be taken into consideration, regardless of whether the people who are living together are of different sexes or the same sex. There is no reason why these people should be disadvantaged merely because they say quite openly, ’I live as I am, and I would like to have the same opportunities and the same options as other people’. There is no reason why we should deviate from the basic principles that we have already adopted here in the European Parliament. We adopted these, as Mr Ullmann has just said, as long ago as February 1994.

  Liikanen , Member of the Commission. – (FI) Mr President, the Commission proposal on which Parliament is now being consulted has a long history behind it. The Commission first forwarded a proposal for consideration by Parliament four years ago, in March 1993. The proposal has two objectives. It seeks firstly to incorporate in the Staff Regulations the principle of equality between men and women, and secondly to make it possible to adopt special positive measures to improve the position of women if conditions are discriminatory from women's point of view.

Parliament delivered its first report in November 1993. On the basis of it, the Commission altered its proposal and incorporated in it the principle of non‐discrimination on grounds of sexual orientation. It was only in March 1996 that I was able to forward the Commission's modified proposal to the Council, because before the Staff Regulations can be amended, extensive consultations are required, particularly with staff representatives. As it was a very long time since the Commission had made and altered its proposal, Parliament asked the Council for a further opportunity to state its views, and this request was granted in October 1996. Now the committee has tabled numerous amendments. They relate both to equality between men and women and to the principle of nondiscrimination on grounds of sexual orientation. I should like to stress that the Commission proposal now under consideration is only a small and partial reform, and not a complete administrative reform. The objective should be to adopt this small and partial reform, now that four years have elapsed, so that we can concentrate on the need for bigger changes.

The debate has mainly focused on two issues, on which I should like to make a few comments. One concerns the age limit and the other the position of cohabiting couples. The proposal suggests that age limits for competitions to recruit staff to the Community institutions be abolished. This idea has been debated over a very long period, and the arguments for and against are well‐known. I should like to stress that the Staff Regulations permit the use of an age limit but do not require it. Thus there is no requirement to operate age limits under the existing rules. It would be justified to reflect about when the use of an age limit is justified and when it is not, and to consider the matter separately from case to case.

One practical problem about which I should be happy to speak to the committee at greater length is the organization of competitions. Most recently in 1993, when a competition for young A‐grade officials was held in twelve Member States, there were 50 000 applicants from the twelve countries with nine languages. Ultimately, 600 were placed on the reserve list – that is, just over 1 %. It was an enormous administrative task and tied up huge amounts of administrative resources. If a similar competition were to be held now, as it shortly will be, the number of applicants from all 15 Member States would no doubt be substantially larger, yet at the same time the number of new jobs will be significantly smaller, because of budgetary constraints. The age limit has been used as one method of controlling the number of applicants. How justified it is is debatable.

On the other hand, I should like to stress that age limits have not always been applied as a recruitment condition. Many temporary staff have been recruited by the Commission without any age limit being applied, and many of them have gone on to become permanent officials through internal competitions. Last year, at my proposal, the Commission approved a new policy on temporary staff, which is as follows: in future, two types of competition are to be held – one type for young generalists and one type for older staff who already have work experience. The intention is that all competitions should be open and public, both those for younger applicants and those for mature candidates, but the mature ones would be required to have specialist knowledge. This procedure has already been used when recruiting in the new Member States. For the more junior A‐grades, young people with little or no work experience were recruited. Separate competitions were held for more mature staff to be employed as A−4s or A−5s, higher up in the hierarchy.

I have just compared the results of these competitions, when recruitment began. Of the young applicants from the new Member States who were placed on the A‐grade reserve lists, 51 % were women. In the more mature age group, only just over 30 % of the applicants placed on the reserve lists were women. This undoubtedly reflects social development. The position of young women is already a good deal stronger now than it was 10 or 20 years ago. But this is a matter which should be considered case by case. I am not sure whether it is essential to abolish all the rules concerning age limits. Each case can be considered on its merits. But as I have said, the Commission is now also opening competitions to more mature applicants.

I shall now turn to the principle of non‐discrimination on sexual grounds. The Commission is aware of the problems of cohabiting couples and the unequal position in which they find themselves, and it has sought solutions to the problem. Last summer, the Commission approved an administrative reform guaranteeing homosexual and heterosexual cohabiting couples all the entitlements enjoyed by married couples which it was possible to grant without amending the Staff Regulations, that is, on the basis of the Regulations as they now stand. I have heard that the European Parliament and the Council are preparing similar reforms. I would encourage Members to keep abreast of these developments.

Parliament now proposes that the Staff Regulations should also be amended so as to entitle unmarried couples to the same social and financial benefits as married couples. This is quite justified from the point of view of equality. But before the Commission submits a proposal to this effect, we need to make a political judgment. What I mean is this: the Council's attitude towards the entitlements of officials has, to put it mildly, been hostile, and it has on a number of occasions proposed substantial cuts. If we propose that cohabiting couples should be placed on a footing of equality with married couples, there is a risk that the Council may respond by abolishing those entitlements enjoyed by married couples which do not exist in many countries. One should reflect carefully on whether to take this risk, because we shall also have to discuss the matter with staff representatives. I do not personally have anything against the idea. I entirely agree that married couples and cohabiting couples should be treated equally. The danger is simply that the Council may take the view that equality should be achieved by abolishing married couples' privileges, thereby securing a position of equality for cohabiting couples.

All in all, I should like to say that, on many points, I agree with Parliament's amendments. But what needs to be decided now is whether to proceed with this small, partial reform, which has already been in the pipeline for four years, or whether to reopen the debate and the consideration of the proposal. One alternative is to take a decision on this small reform now and then go on to prepare a complete revision of the Staff Regulations, which in many respects needs to be undertaken before enlargement. Unfortunately, the timetable for dealing with such matters is enormously protracted. This proposal has taken four years: how long will it take if we embark upon a complete reform? It might be a good idea to discuss this with the committee. Even just internal negotiations and consultations with staff organizations take a huge amount of time. The procedures are lengthy. If something is to be done, it should be done in such a way as to be completed within the lifetime of the present Parliament and Commission. May I thank the committee for the interest it has taken in the issue; I shall be happy to continue the discussions on this and related matters later.

  Pex (PPE). – (NL) Mr President, I put three clear questions to the Commissioner and I have not had answers to them. After two and a half years I think I am entitled to some. The Commissioner says he agrees with the amendments, but he does nothing further. The Commission has the right of initiative. Let us now see the Commissioner doing something, and let him at least give me an answer to my questions.

I will repeat them: one, what will the Commission do if Parliament adopts the amendments tomorrow? Two, how long will it take for the Staff Regulations to be amended and, three, why are the laws and constitutions of those countries which do have equal rights not respected by the European institutions, by the European Commission? Please, Mr President, will you see to it that Parliament receives answers to these three specific questions.

  Liikanen , Member of the Commission. – (FI) Mr President, I have just said that we ought to reflect on whether or not to move towards a decision on this partial reform at all. If there is a desire to do so, a decision should be taken on the proposal as it stands. If we want to reopen the whole subject, we shall probably have to go through a procedure which will take a couple of years longer to complete. In compliance with the principle of cooperation with staff, we shall have to enter into fresh consultations on this whole subject. The Commission needs to think about this. Personally I would be inclined – although this is a matter for the Commission to decide – to take a decision today to approve this small reform; the views stated by Parliament could then be taken into account when considering the Staff Regulations as a whole.

Another point which is legally problematic is this: can the Staff Regulations of the European Communities treat nationals of different Member States differently? So far, legal experts have held that the Staff Regulations must treat all officials equally. They do not provide any legal scope for discriminating among officials on the basis of their nationality. This is what the experts say, and since this is the legal interpretation, it is difficult for the Commission to deviate from it. As Member States' views change – we do now have a couple of countries which have granted rights, and made arrangements for registration, for cohabiting couples and particularly for homosexual couples – this of course improves the chances of getting changes through the Council. I should be very happy if the Member States' views developed in such a way that things could be put in order in this respect.

The third point to which I have drawn attention is the unavoidable need for a political judgment as to whether we should propose equality for married and unmarried couples. Is the Commission ready to run the risk that, as a result, married couples' existing privileges, particularly those of a financial nature, may be abolished and equality may be secured in this way? Many people may regard this as a simple matter, but I can assure you that from the point of view of the staff organizations the issue is a very delicate one.

  President . – Thank you, Mr Commissioner.

The debate is closed.

The vote will be taken at noon tomorrow.(1)

Annex

I. Questions to the Council

 

Question No 23 by Birgitta Ahlqvist (H−0031/97)

Subject: Events in Belarus

The events which took place in Belarus in November 1996 showed that President Lukashenka was not prepared to compromise with the parliamentary opposition. He effectively limited the freedom of opinion. His total control over the media gave him a monopoly over political news in rural areas, and he was thus able to rally the voters to his support.

On 1 November, I put a question to the Council concerning its view of developments in Belarus. The answer I received was that it would wait and see what the referendum at the end of the month brought. Now we see the result: the President was not interested in compromise with the opposition and opted for an authoritarian solution.

How has the Council signalled its concern at the evolution of authoritarian presidential powers in Belarus? How does it intend to proceed as regards its relations with Belarus? What contact has been made with the opposition in order to support it in its dialogue with the President?

Answer

The evolution of the political situation in Belarus has brought the EU to use a number of diplomatic steps (demarches and EU statements) in the run‐up to the referendum.

After the referendum on a new Constitution carried out on 24 November 1996 the EU Troika, headed by Prime Minister Bruton, has conveyed the Union's grave concerns about the evolution in Belarus in a meeting with President Lukashenko in the margins of the OSCE Summit of Lisbon. The Union has at this occasion mainly stressed the necessity of respecting procedures foreseen in the Belarus Constitution for modifying it and notably to respect the constitutional rights of the Belarusian Parliament; furthermore, the means of preparing and organising the referendum, like access to the media and full information for voters, as well as several provisions of the Constitution submitted to the referendum, were put into question.

The Council pressed its proposal to send a fact finding mission to Belarus in order to obtain an objective understanding of the developments. President Lukashenko eventually acceded to this request. A delegation headed by a former Justice Minister of the Netherlands as Presidency and comprising constitutional experts and diplomats elected by the Presidency, Commission, OECD and Council of Europe visited Minsk from January 26‐31. It conducted some 60 interviews with government, opposition, media and other figures, and was received by President Lukashenko. The mission's findings have been the subject of two COEST Working Group meetings and will form the basis for deliberations of the General Affairs Council on February 24.

Furthermore, it must be stressed that since November 1996 contacts with opposition leaders and the representatives of the ‚shadow’ Parliament and its speaker as well as other leaders of the civil society have been constantly maintained.

Question No 24 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0038/97)

Subject: Findings by a German court concerning Mrs Ciller's involvement in drugs trafficking

Article K.1 of the Treaty on European Union states that the responsibilities of the Council of Ministers shall include cooperation '...for the purposes of preventing and combating...unlawful drug trafficking and other serious forms of international crime, including if necessary certain aspects of customs cooperation'. Article K.5 provides that Member States shall defend the common positions adopted in these matters in international organizations and international conferences.

What measures does the Council intend to take in respect of the Turkish Government, in view of the public accusations made by the President and Judge of a Frankfurt court, in the wake of a major trial involving heroin trafficking by Turkish gangs, that the latter were operating under the protection of members of the Turkish Government, and in particular the Turkish Foreign Minister, Tansu Ciller? What specific measures does the Council intend to take to ensure that the Customs Union with Turkey does not lead to an increase in the trafficking of drugs from that country, particularly in the light of the involvement of members of the Turkish Government in this trafficking?

Answer

The points mentioned by the Honourable Member of Parliament have not been discussed in the framework of the Council.

Trafficking on Balkan routes is a major concern for the Council. As an implementing measure of the Drugs plan adopted by the meeting of the European Council of Madrid a Joint Action was agreed under Irish Presidency concerning participation by the Member States of the European Union in the strategic operation planned by the Customs Co‐operation Council (CCC) to combat drug smuggling on the Balkan route.

Moreover the Council at its meeting of 28 / 29 November 1996 took note of the report of the European Drugs Unit on drug trafficking activities by Turkish criminal organisations from an EU perspective.

The operational follow‐up to recommendations in this report is being discussed in competent bodies of the Council.

Question No 25 by Claude Desama (H−0039/97)

Subject: Use of regional aid for relocation purposes

The Belgian undertaking Boston Scientific Europe which is located in an Objective 2 area is to be transferred to an Objective 1 area in Ireland. This case of relocation clearly reveals serious flaws in the European system of aid which encourages competition between regions in decline. It also shows the urgent need for a fiscal harmonization policy in Europe. How does the Dutch Presidency intend to address this problem?

Answer

As the Honourable Member knows, aid under the Structural Funds is governed by the regulations adopted by the Council in July 1993.

By the terms of these same regulations, the Council entrusted the management of the Structural Funds to the Commission. Consequently, it is no longer appropriate for the Council to comment on the way the Funds are implemented or the consequences.

In regard to a fiscal harmonization policy as mentioned by the Honourable Member, I would remind him that the Dublin European Summit took due note of the Commission's report on developments in the European Union's tax systems, stressing that work should continue in this area. The summit welcomed the Commission's intention to continue discussions within a working party on fiscal policy.

Question No 26 by Sören Wibe (H−0042/97)

Subject: Egg industry aid which is distorting competition

Sweden's egg producers have been hard hit since the country joined the EU, the reason being that Finnish egg producers receive aid (so far authorized for the period 1 January 1995 to 31 December 1999) which is paid directly to them from the Finnish state. The aid was actually intended to help cut production and eliminate Finland;s egg surplus. This means that Finnish packers are able to buy eggs from producers at low prices and sell them more cheaply than Swedish packers can. Swedish packers have to pay the whole production cost to Swedish egg producers, who do not receive any production aid. In other words, the 'advantageous' price of Finnish eggs is the result of distorted competition.

This has resulted in heavy losses for Swedish egg producers, some of whom have gone bankrupt. If nothing is done to tackle this problem, the Swedish egg industry could very quickly be wiped out. Does the Council intend to take any steps to deal with the problem?

Answer

Finland's Accession Treaty to the European Union included special measures in the field of state aid, in particular for egg producers. These measures were intended to take account of the need to bring Finnish prices into line with Community prices from the very first day of membership and, at the same time, to improve the structural situation of this sector in Finland. These measures are laid down in Article 138 of the Treaty of Accession and also in Article 140 of Annex XIV to this same Treaty.

Article 138 states that Finland may grant, subject to authorisation from the Commission and for a transitional period of five years from the date of membership, transitional and progressively reduced state aid to producers of basic agricultural produce which is subject to the Common Agricultural Policy. Eggs come into this category. This same article also lays down the conditions for the authorisation of this aid by the Commission, for the determining of the amount of aid, and for any adaptations which may be necessary as a result of developments in the Common Agricultural Policy and in Community price levels.

Article 140, together with Annex XIV, stipulates that for a transitional period of five years from the date of accession, the Commission authorises Finland to grant transitional state aid in the form of investment aid, subject to the following conditions: this aid must not result in an increase in global production capacities and must respect the individual production limits laid down by the Commission according to the procedures laid down in Article 29 of Regulation 4253/88/EEC.

The Accession Treaty also gives the Commission the option of adopting protective measures at the request of the Member State concerned in the event of serious disturbances resulting from trade in the area of agriculture between one or more of the new Member States and the Community as it was composed on 31 December 1994, or between the new Member States themselves.

It is therefore the Commission's responsibility to ensure that the provisions of Articles 138 and 140 of the Treaty of Accession are correctly applied and also to check that the conditions justifying the application of the protective clause are met.

***

Question No 27 by Hans Lindqvist (H−0046/97)

Subject: Sweden and EMU

An intense debate is currently in progress in Sweden concerning the country's possible participation in economic and monetary union (EMU). The Swedish government takes the view that it is the Riksdag which in autumn 1997 will decide whether or not Sweden participates in EMU. Sweden's Prime Minister, Göran Persson, has expressed fears that EMU will result in a common fiscal and financial policy.

What would the consequences for Sweden be if the Riksdag says 'no' in autumn 1997 to Swedish entry into EMU? Is there a desire to establish a common fiscal and financial policy for the EU?

Answer

In reply to the first part of the Honourable Member's question, the Council points out that Article 109 J of the Treaty on European Union states that if, at the end of 1997, the date for entry to third stage of EMU has not been set, as will no doubt be the case, it will then start on 1 January 1999.

Before 1 July 1998 the Council, meeting in the composition of the Heads of State or Government, must confirm, in accordance with the procedure laid down in Article 109 J, which Member States fulfil the necessary conditions for the adoption of the single currency.

In accordance with Article 109 K of the Treaty, the Council must also, acting on a Commission recommendation, decide whether any Member States shall have derogation because they do not yet fulfil the conditions for the adoption of a single currency.

As to the second part of the question, I would like to stress that the Council cannot pronounce on the consequences of a possible decision by the Swedish Parliament which, furthermore, is a hypothetical question. At the present stage, the Treaty does not include any obligation for a rigorously uniform fiscal and financial policy.

***

Question No 28 by Luciano Pettinari (H−0050/97)

Subject: 'Colonization' of Famagusta

The Turkish terrorist organization 'Grey Wolves' which was responsible for the murder of two Greek Cypriots last summer has threatened, both in declarations made by the head of the organization in Cyprus and in a communiqué published by Turkish‐Cypriot newspapers, to establish settlers in the occupied City of Famagusta.

In view of UN Security Council Resolution 550 (1984) which states that the Security Council considers inadmissible any attempt to settle in any part of Varosha persons other than the inhabitants of this sector, and requests that this sector be placed under the administration of the United Nations, does the Council intend to intervene to counter the threats made by Turkish extremist circles, which are seeking to obtain the partition of the island by creating the erroneous impression that peaceful coexistence between the two communities on the island is impossible, and to exert pressure on Turkey to secure the withdrawal of all the occupying forces from the island (European Parliament resolution of 24.10.1996)(1) and compliance with the relevant European Parliament and the UN Security Council resolutions?

Answer

Statements like the one referred to by the Honourable Member and recently taken up the Turkish‐Cypriot leader Mr R Denktash himself, together with the tragic events of last summer in Cyprus, have again highlighted the urgent need to intensify efforts towards promoting a comprehensive political settlement in Cyprus, under the aegis of the United Nations.

The Council has called for restraint and avoidance by all concerned of any actions liable to raise tension and to hinder or complicate talks between the parties promoted by the international community for later this year.

The Council has reiterated its full support for the work of the UN Secretary‐General and his Special Representative for Cyprus, Professor Han Sung‐Joo, and has called on all parties to step up their efforts to make a decisive move towards a settlement on the basis of a bi‐communal and bi‐zonal Federation in accordance with the relevant United Nations Security Council Resolutions and High Level Agreements of 1977 and 1979.

The prospect of membership of the European Union could be used constructively to make progress towards a just and viable solution. It is vital that this opportunity be grasped.

The Netherlands Presidency has invited Ambassador Heaslip to continue in his task as Presidency Representative for Cyprus. He will keep in close contact with the parties in the region and other international players, in particular the UN.

The Council will continue to urge Turkey to contribute to a solution in Cyprus in accordance with the UN Security Council resolutions and the High Level Agreements of 1977 and 1979.

Question No 29 by Arthur Newens (H−0054/97)

Subject: Aid and human rights in relation to the Republic of Yemen

With regard to the substantial aid package from the European Communities to the Republic of Yemen, will the Council undertake to maintain strong pressure on the authorities of that country, as a condition of the aid package, to abide by international undertakings and to seek national reconciliation, with particular reference to the restitution of the confiscated property of southerners and the right to return in security of approximately 1, 250 political refugees from the former southern regime and the conduct of the proposed elections later this year to internationally acceptable standards?

Answer

Since 1988 the Yemeni Government has repeatedly stated that it would be in favour of negotiating a new Agreement. During the 7th EC‐Yemen Joint Co‐operation Committee meeting in Sanaa on 19 September 1996, the Community agreed with the Yemeni side to start talks on a new co‐operation agreement as soon as possible.

Therefore, the Commission has proposed to the Council to negotiate a new agreement with Yemen (replacing the 1984 Co‐operation Agreement) which would be modelled in accordance with the latest generation of the EC's cooperation agreements with Asian developing countries (i.e. Bangladesh, Cambodia, Laos, Pakistan). The Council is currently examining the Draft Directives with a view to adopt them on 24 February 1997. Of course, under the information procedure governing the relations with the Parliament in the field of the association and trade agreements, the relevant parliamentary committees will be duly informed on the content of the mandate as soon as it has been adopted by the Council.

The principle of the EC‐Yemen Co‐operation Agreement would be to enhance and develop the co‐operation between the Contracting Parties in the areas which fall within the bounds of their respective competence. Therefore, the draft mandate for the Agreement would include development, trade, economic and cultural cooperation, environmental protection and sustainable management of natural resources as well as human rights and democracy.

In particular, the development co‐operation will remain an important component of overall co‐operation between the Parties. In order to emphasize the importance of human rights and democracy, the proposed Co‐operation Agreement should accordingly include articles on human rights and democracy and the need for human resource development. The standard clause stating respect for human rights and democratic principles, will be the basis for the co‐operation between the Parties and for the provisions of the agreement, thus constituting an essential element of the Agreements.

Since the start of EC‐Yemen co‐operation in 1978, 57, 7 MECU have been committed for economic and development projects in Yemen. The Commission has committed more from 27 MECU since Yemen unification in 1990 for 15 economic or development co‐operation projects and project studies. In addition, approximately 10 MECU were spent on food aid, humanitarian aid and decentralised co‐operation projects.

The Government of Yemen has invited the EU Member States and the European Parliament, to participate in the monitoring of the 27 April general elections. Member States have responded positively and the Presidency will ensure co‐ordination of their task.

***

Question No 30 by Kirsten Jensen (H−0063/97)

Subject: Procedures for authorizing the marketing of genetically engineered maize

On 18 December 1996, the Commission decided to authorize the marketing of genetically engineered maize produced by Ciba‐Geigy, in accordance with the provisions of Directive 90/220/EC(2) and on the basis of reports by three scientific committees (on food, animal nutrition and pesticides). The decision was taken despite opposition from a large majority of Member States (13 out of 15) and the European Parliament.

The Commissioner responsible for the environment told Parliament's environment committee on 21 January 1997 that the above procedure was 'unsatisfactory'. Will the Council act to ensure that the IGC abolishes this remarkable procedure which leaves an awful lot to be desired regards democracy? If this matter does not concern the IGC but is of an interinstitutional or similar nature, will the Council take the necessary action?

Answer

Although it is not for the Council to comment on statements made by a member of the Commission, and the way the question is formulated does not permit it to identify the procedure to which the Honourable Member is alluding, the Council would point out that:

‐ Decision 97/98/EC, adopted by the Commission on 23 January 1997 and concerning the marketing of genetically engineered maize, was taken in accordance with Directive 90/220/EEC (adopted by the Council in cooperation with the European Parliament) and Article 13 in particular;

‐ Directive 90/220/EEC, and in particular Articles 13 and 21, is categoric regarding the division of decision‐making powers between the Council and the Commission depending on the various stages in the process leading to a decision to authorize marketing.

In any event, the Council has no competence to intervene in the activities of the IGC. If, however, the Commission presented a proposal to amend Directive 90/220/EEC, the Council would most certainly look at this as soon as possible.

***

Question No 31 by Konstadinos Klironomos (H−0067/97)

Subject: Recommendation issued by UK authorities to British tourists intending to visit Greece

The Foreign Office recently issued a 'recommendation' to British tourists to avoid Greece as a tourist destination, owing to strike action in that country.

Given the risk that this recommendation may discourage British tourists from spending their holidays in Greece, and that Greece is not the only country affected by strikes (quite apart from the fact that terrorist attacks occur in other Member States of the European Union on an almost daily basis), will the Council say whether it considers that this 'recommendation', which will have a particularly damaging effect on Greece's ailing tourist sector, is justified or exaggerated, bearing in mind that this is a particularly critical period, since most bookings for the summer of 1997 will be made from now on, and what measures it intends to take to ensure that Greece does not suffer the consequences of this 'recommendation', i.e. a drop in the number of UK tourists to Greece?

Answer

The Council has no knowledge of a recommendation by the UK Foreign Office concerning British tourists intending to travel to Greece.

Clearly any recommendation of this kind issued by an individual Member State is issued on its own behalf and in no way commits the Council which is not authorised to comment on statements made by Member States outside the context of its meetings.

Question No 32 by Richard Howitt (H−0068/97)

Subject: Oil exploration by European companies in Colombia

What active steps has the Council taken following Parliament's 24 October 1996 resolution(3) and, the Colombian Attorney‐General's Official Inquiry into the activities of British Petroleum in the Casanare region of Colombia? Bearing in mind its answer to Question No 32 H−1018/96, (4) will the Council now commit itself to making a positive intervention to pursue the aims of the resolution?

Answer

The Council has continued to follow closely the situation in Colombia. It is very concerned about the continuing high level of violence, which has resulted in death and injury of civilians. It will continue to make known its concerns in relation to human rights, democracy and the rule of law on every possible occasion, as it has done in the past. The Union, of course, supports the path of dialogue as the only way to resolve differences; unfortunately, too many people in Colombia appear ready to resort to arms, in this context BP's request that the Colombian Attorney General opens an inquiry into their activities is the United Nations Human Rights Office in Bogotá. We urge the UN High Commissioner for Human Rights to accelerate the establishment of the office, so that it can do its job.

On the question of the fight against drugs, the Honourable Member will be aware that the Dublin European Council endorsed the implementation of the agreed follow‐up to the report of the expert mission to Latin‐american. This will continue to be the subject of contacts with Latin American countries, including Colombia.

Question No 33 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0070/97)

Subject: Attention to Euro‐Mediterranean policy

What steps does the Netherlands Presidency intend to take to counteract the current deceleration and incipient decline of Euro‐Mediterranean policy?

Answer

The Council has on a number of occasions reiterated the importance it attaches to relations between the European Union and its mediterranean partners, a view which is, we know, fully shared by the European Parliament. In this context, the Euro‐Mediterranean Conference which took place in Barcelona in November 1995 marked a turning point in these relations, establishing a partnership covering three areas: strengthened political dialogue, the development of economic and financial co‐operation, and greater emphasis on the social, cultural and human dimension.

Since the Barcelona Conference, a large number of activities have taken place which have helped to consolidate the development of this partnership. In particular meetings at ministerial level have been held covering such issues as Industry, Culture, Information Society, Fisheries and Water. Under the political and security partnership, work on implementation of the principles in the Barcelona Declaration has progressed, notably through agreement on a number of confidence and security building measures, with a view to the conclusion of a Euro‐mediterranean Charter/Pact as an overall framework.

The Council considers that the number and range of activities which have taken place throughout last year constitute substantial progress in the development of the partnership. The Council has welcomed the plans to consolidate this further through the programme of work over the coming months. The Dutch Presidency, with the full involvement of all member states, will work hard to ensure that the momentum achieved since the Barcelona meeting is maintained and developed. In this context, the next meeting of Foreign Ministers in Malta on 15‐16 April will be a priority for the Presidency and for the Council as a whole. It will provide the opportunity for ministers to assess the progress achieved so far, and to target priorities for the future.

The multilateral element of the partnership is complemented by the network of bilateral agreements currently being established. Agreements with Tunisia, Israel and Morocco have already ben signed, and negotiations are being pursued actively with Egypt, Jordan and Lebanon. An agreement with the Palestinians is due to be signed shortly. Negotiations with Algeria are expected to start soon, and it has been agreed that exploratory conversations will be held with Syria. The Dutch Presidency will do all it can to ensure that the outstanding negotiations are concluded as soon as possible in order to make further progress towards the completion of the network of new agreements.

Question No 34 by Bernd Posselt (H−0071/97)

Subject: EU office in Kosovo

The European Parliament has called for an EU office to be opened in Kosovo, the idea being that such European representation could prevent a dangerous conflict in advance.

Has the Council already initiated preparations for this, or what else does it intend to do to prevent conflict in Kosovo?

Answer

As the Honourable Member will be aware, the Council has repeatedly expressed its concern about the situation in Kosovo. At its sessions of 1 and 28 October 1996, it reaffirmed its conviction that an increased international presence in Kosovo would have a moderating effect on the two parties and could thus contribute to reducing tensions. An international presence would certainly constitute another positive element in finding an acceptable solution to the question of the status of Kosovo within the FRY. To that end, the Council pursues the aim of establishing an EU presence in Kosovo and continues to insist on that point with the authorities of the FRY.

With regard to the second part of the question, the European Union has made clear that the opening of an EU office in Kosovo and the accreditation of an Ambassador of the FRY to the European Union are being dealt with in parallel.

Question No 35 by Fiorella Ghilardotti (H−0073/97)

Subject: The Council's property policy

Shortly after moving into new premises large enough to house virtually all its staff, the Council Secretariat realized that the accession of new Member States would necessitate building an extension on the adjacent plot of land on Chaussée d'Etterbeek.

This plot, previously the property of the Régie des bâtiments (State buildings authority), had recently been sold to a developer.

Why, in the interests of sound financial management, did the Council not acquire this land in advance, thus leaving itself free to build without having to go through the intermediary of a developer?

Answer

The Council Secretariat informed the Belgian authorities back in 1994 of its interest in the plot of land on the Chaussée d'Etterbeek in Brussels, with a view to extending the Justus Lipsius building.

The Council Secretariat then discovered in a press article that the plot of land was going to be sold.

The Council has asked the Secretariat to provide a full report on this subject and will soon be discussing the matter.

Subject: Bodies of schoolgirls found in sea off Sri Lanka

Is the Council prepared to call for an impartial investigation into the finding of bodies of schoolgirls inside bags dumped in the sea off the coast of Mullaitivu, on the Jaffna peninsula of Sri Lanka?

Answer

It would be inappropriate for the Council to involve itself in criminal cases in any or every part of the world unless they raised policy issues for the European Union.

Question No 37 by Karin Riis‐jørgensen (H−0080/97)

Subject: Council declarations annexed to legislative texts

Having regard to Parliament's resolution of 10 October 1995 (B4‐1204/95) on the transparency of Council decisions and the Community's legislative procedures(5) and following the adoption of the Code of Conduct of 2 October 1995 on the publishing of Council minutes and statements in the minutes, in which particular cases has the Council continued its practice of making unilateral statements on pending legislation in its minutes, for what reasons, have they made accessible to the public in accordance with the Code of Conduct and has it always obtained the consent of Parliament in advance in the case of declarations made under the codecision procedure, or, at least informed Parliament as co‐legislator?

Answer

The Council adopted 301 legislative acts between 2 October 1995 ‐the date on which the Council adopted the Code of Conduct on the publishing of minutes and statements in the minutes of the Council acting as legislator – and 31 December 1996. These acts gave rise to 537 statements in the minutes.

Of the 301 legislative acts, 152 were not the subject of statements in the minutes. We can therefore judge that the Council made a moderate use of the instrument of statements in the minutes.

The Council decided to publish 534 of the 537 statements in the minutes, using the aforementioned Code of Conduct.

As stated in the Council's written reply to written question P−2385/96 which Mr Bonde asked on this same subject, only three statements made when adopting legislative texts have not been published in the framework of the Code of Conduct for the period indicated. This is because these statements, all three of which concerned the agreement on shipbuilding in the framework of the OECD, did not relate to legislative acts as such but rather to the conclusion of an international agreement.

Moreover, one of these three statements – that by the Commission when adopting Council Regulation 3094/95 on aid to shipbuilding – was submitted to the Honourable Member by the Council following his request of 23 September 1996, pursuant to Council Decision 93/731/EC on public access to Council documents.

Would the Honourable Member please note that the Code of Conduct concerns the final adoption of legislative acts, in the sense given to this term in the annex to the Council's Rules of Procedure. Statements in the minutes made when the Council adopts joint positions, in the sense of Article 189 B and 189 C of the EC Treaty, are not made accessible to the public. They are only made accessible at the time of the final adoption of the legislative act.

Question No 38 by Christine Oddy (H−0082/97)

Subject: Nicaragua

Is the Council aware that the new government of the Liberal Alliance in Nicaragua has rejected the Minimum Agenda which was signed by all political parties, except the Liberal Alliance, and by many popular organizations before the election? It pledged them to support and respect the minimum international standards on rights enshrined in international treaties. Will the Council press the new government to adhere to international minimum rights?

Answer

The Council has not discussed the rejection by the new Government of Nicaragua of the Minimum Agenda referred to by the honourable Member.

Irrespective of whether the party of the new Government of Nicaragua has signed the Minimum Agenda or not, it is one of the duties of a government to honour its undertakings with respect to international treaties and covenants, to which it is a party.

In that context, the Union is sure that the new Government of Nicaragua will abide by its international obligations.

Question No 39 by Francisco Sanz Fernández (H−0089/97)

Subject: 'European cities of culture' after the year 2000

The Council has asked the Commission to draw up and submit a proposal for establishing a procedure for the designation of 'European cities of culture' after the year 2000.

However, at the most recent formal meeting of the Council of Culture Ministers, on 16 December 1996, France and Italy put forward Lille and Genoa respectively as candidates for 2001, and Austria proposed Graz for 2003.

Can the Council state what cities apart from the above have to date been officially proposed by the national delegations as candidates for 'European city of culture', and for which years from 2001 on?

How does the Council explain the fact that just when a new set of rules is being prepared on the matter the Member States are continuing to put forward individual proposals for 'European city of culture' for 2001 and later years?

Answer

The following candidacies have to date been officially submitted for designation as ‚European city of culture’ after the year 2000:

‐ 2001: Basle and Riga (submitted as candidates in 1995)

‐ 2003: Graz (submitted as candidate in December 1996)

It is not for the Council to intervene in decisions taken by the authorities in the Member States, especially as, while awaiting the new regulations to be adopted on the basis of a Commission proposal, the field in question continues to be governed by the procedure applied to date.

Question No 40 by Gerard Collins (H−0090/97)

Subject: Air safety agency

Has any Member State proposed that the new air safety agency discussed at the Transport Council on 12‐13 December 1996 be established on its territory and would the Council view favourably the location of this new agency in Shannon in Ireland?

Answer

The Commission submitted to the Council on 16 December 1996 a recommendation for a Council Decision authorizing the Commission to start negotiations with a view to establishing a European organization responsible for civil aviation safety.

The idea is to create an authority in Europe, which should be able to adopt safety rules and to check conformity of products, organization and personnel with these rules.

The recommendation by the Commission is being examined by the Council bodies and efforts will be made to maintain or enhance the high level of safety standards in aviation prevailing in Europe.

In the introduction to its recommendation, the Commission recalled that, since it would not be appropriate in the wider European context to give a Community agency the power to issue its own safety rules, the best way was to create a new international organization with appropriate powers to enable it to fulfil its tasks. This new organization should be based on the present Joint Aviation Authorities (JAA).

Until the adoption of a Decision by the Council on this issue, in particular on the statutory framework of the European organization responsible for civil aviation safety, a discussion on the location of its headquarters should be considered as premature. However, no Member State proposed that such a new organization should be established on its own territory or in any specific location.

***

Question No 41 by Pat Gallagher (H−0095/97)

Subject: Single currency and overseas investment

Has the Council considered the implications of a single currency involving some, but not all, EU countries for the future pattern of investment by overseas multinational companies into job‐creating projects in EU countries, as highlighted recently by the President of Toyota concerning investment in Britain, and does the Council consider that overseas companies are likely to concentrate their EU investments inside or outside a single currency zone?

Answer

According to Article 3a(2) of the Treaty, the introduction of a single currency and the definition and the conduct of a single monetary policy and exchange rate policy has as a primary objective to maintain price stability and to support the general economic policies in the Community, in accordance with the principle of an open market economy with free competition.

The single monetary policy therefore does not seek to influence as such investments from third countries or to concentrate them in Member States inside or outside the single currency zone.

Furthermore, monetary considerations are but one factor an investor has to take into account when deciding where to invest.

***

Question No 42 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0099/97)

Subject: Implementing shipping safety rules

Recent accidents involving scheduled maritime transport vessels and cargo vessels flying Community flags which endangered human lives, such as the recent accidents to the car and passenger ferry 'SYROS EXPRESS' which drifted at sea for more than five hours because of a power cut or that involving the cargo vessel 'DYSTOS' in which numerous lives were lost, make it imperative to examine the question of controls on the implementation of shipping safety rules by the relevant authorities. For example, there is the case of the Greek Registration Organisation: following privatisation in 1992, 13 vessels with seaworthiness certificates sank with the loss of 49 lives. Serious questions should also be raised about the reliability of modern methods of transmitting SOS signals and the abolishment of the post of wireless operator from crews.

Will the Council say what initiatives it intends to take to improve shipping safety rules in order to ensure that proper controls are carried out on vessels before seaworthiness certificates are issued, particularly where these are issued by private individuals, and to ensure that these individuals are also monitored, and finally whether it intends to reintroduce the post of wireless operator in crews on a mandatory basis, at least until the new safety and communications systems have proved their reliability?

Answer

Regarding the Council's intentions for action in the field of maritime safety, the Council would like to indicate from the outset that it has adopted several resolutions containing action programmes for the prevention of maritime casualties off the European coasts and of incidents of marine pollution. Numerous directives and regulations have implemented these action programmes and more measures are to come.

Concerning the three sub‐questions made in the parliamentary question, the Council would like to indicate the following:

The Council tried to improve the quality of navigability certificates and the construction of ships by adopting Directive 94/57/EC on common rules and standards for ship inspection and survey organizations and for the relevant activities of maritime administrations. Under this Directive, each ship must be constructed and maintained in accordance with the requirements of a classification organization; those organizations must meet high standards of professional competence and shall be under the strict control of the national authorities and of the Commission.

The issue of the possible deficiencies in the modern techniques for broadcasting distress messages, which were introduced by the Global Maritime Distress and Safety System (GMDSS), has never been discussed by the Council. The Council also understands from the Commission's reply to Question P−597/96 put by Mr EPHREMIDIS, that the Commission does not intend to propose changing the GMDSS system.

The Council also cannot not take up positions on the opportuneness of the abolishment of the function of the radio operator. The Council has never discussed this matter and induces from the already mentioned Commission reply that this Institution has no intention of proposing to re‐instate the function of radio‐officer.

As already indicated in its reply to Question E−0435/95, put by
Mr EPHREMIDIS, GMDSS provided that the function of radio‐electric officer could be taken up by any qualified deck officer; Directive 94/58/EC on the minimum level of training of seafarers tries to contribute to the efficiency of that system. This directive, which copies into Community law the International Convention of 1977 on Standards for Safety, Training, Certification and Watchkeeping (STCW), requires every master, chief mate and deck officer to be trained in morse, signalling, radio communication procedures and use of radio equipment.

Question No 43 by Nikolaos Papakyriazis (H−0100/97/rev. 1)

Subject: Anti‐Semitism – extradition of Alois Brunner

According to past and recent revelations in the international press, the war criminal Alois Brunner who is responsible for the slaughter of 56 000 Jews from Thessaloniki is living in Damascus in Syria.

Brunner, a former SS officer, who has been on the wanted list since 1945 and is sought by the German authorities, was sentenced to death in France for the extermination of 25 000 Jews in Drancy. Interpol has issued a warrant for his arrest, but so far without success. Syria denies that Brunner is on its territory, and Germany is hesitating to call for his extradition.

What measures has the Council taken so far in the Brunner case, and what does it intend to do, within the framework of its powers, to clear up this particular affair and secure justice for the thousands of victims?

Bearing in mind that 1997 has been designated European Year against Racism, Anti‐Semitism and Xenophobia, and that the last few years have seen an alarming upsurge of racism and anti‐Semitism frequently culminating in attempts to deny that the holocaust took place, what measures has the Council taken – or does it intend to take in future – to ensure that the EU and its Member States react vigorously to phenomena of this kind?

Answer

The Council is aware of the problems mentioned by the Honourable Member of the European Parliament. Concerning the European Union and its Member States, the Florence European Council on 21 and 22 June 1996 reaffirmed the Union's determination to combat racism and xenophobia with the utmost resolve.

The Council adopted, on 15 July 1996, a Joint Action on the basis of Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union, concerning action to combat racism and xenophobia. This Joint Action sets out to facilitate international judicial cooperation in combating racism and xenophobia with provision either for certain racist and xenophobic behaviour to be made a criminal offence or, failing that and pending any adoption of the necessary legislation, for the principle of double criminality to be waived.

At its meeting in Dublin on 13 and 14 December 1996, the European Council welcomed the progress made for the establishment of a European Monitoring Centre on Racism and Xenophobia. It called on the Council to complete its work with a view to the speedy establishment of this Centre which will have to work in close cooperation with the Council of Europe. The establishment of the Centre will be an important element to counter racist and xenophobic phenomena and will mark the importance of 1997 as the European Year against racism and xenophobia.

For the problems relating to extradition, two Conventions have been signed to simplify extradition procedures between the Member States of the European Union: one Convention was singed on 10 March 1995, the other was signed on 27 September 1996. These Conventions will facilitate the measures to combat racism and xenophobia within the European Union.

II. Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 62 by Bernd Posselt (H−0072/97)

Subject: EU research policy

It seems that research cooperation within the EU is in serious decline because of financial constraints.

What new priorities will the Commission adopt in 1997 to counteract this?

Answer

It is true that public research is subject to strict budgetary constraints in the Union Member States. We are entitled to be concerned at such a development when experience shows that research – and cooperative research in particular – helps to meet the social and economic challenges facing the Community.

It is through the Research and Technological Development (RTD) Framework Programmes that the Community promotes cooperation. These activities are attracting an increasing number of researchers and are resulting in the creation of fast‐growing cooperation networks. The decision for a 4th RTD Framework Programme makes it possible to guarantee the continuity of Community action through to 1998.

The Commission has defined the future framework programme's principal areas of activity, within which cooperative research activities will be maintained. Community action will concentrate on six priority topics and will be structured as follows: three thematic programmes and three horizontal programmes. It seems that the content of these programmes will be more clearly defined and take more account of citizens' expectations. Each of the three thematic programmes will be organized at two levels: a limited number of key actions designed to stimulate research around important social and economic objectives for the Community, and general activities for the development of generic fundamental research technologies in order to maintain the development of knowledge and skills.

On 12 February 1997 the Commission adopted a working document to this effect and plans to hold a wide‐ranging debate with Parliament and other Community bodies from 28 February to 1 March next.

***

Question No 63 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0088/97)

Subject: Scientific cooperation with the Maghreb countries

What international scientific cooperation agreements will be concluded with the Maghreb countries, and through what scientific and technical activities will cooperation be pursued? What opportunities will be afforded for associated Maghreb countries to become involved in research, technological development, and innovation policies?

Answer

Research cooperation is included in the cooperation agreements concluded in 1992 between the European Community and the Maghreb countries, and also in the new Euro‐Mediterranean agreements already signed or being negotiated. No specific scientific and technological cooperation agreements with these countries are therefore being planned at the present time.

The Maghreb countries have been involved in the Community's research activities since 1983, initially through the ‚Development sciences and technologies programme’, subsequently through a specific cooperation initiative with the Mediterranean countries (the Avicenne Programme) and, more recently, in the framework of the 4th Framework Programme's specific international cooperation programme.

Cooperation with the Maghreb countries takes the form of joint research projects, cooperation networks and scientific conferences. Maghreb researchers have participated in all these activities.

This cooperation should significantly expand in the context of the Euro‐Mediterranean partnership. The working document on the 5th Framework Programme, adopted by the Commission on 12 February, stresses the priority which should be awarded to international technological and scientific cooperation, and cooperation with Central European and Mediterranean countries.

The three principal areas for this increased cooperation should be the carrying out of joint research activities, the reinforcement of local research capacities and technology transfer. The Commission will be providing details of the aims and means of Euro‐Mediterranean technological and scientific cooperation in a special communication on the subject which it will be submitting in the course of the coming weeks.

Question No 64 by Hugh Kerr (H−0102/97)

Subject: Arts education and training (SOCRATES programme)

Will arts education and training continue to be supported under the SOCRATES programme?

Answer

As in the past, the Commission will continue, as far as possible, to support cooperation activities in connection with arts education when they fall within the programme's objectives and meet the criteria which govern the various actions of the SOCRATES programme.

Culture is most certainly an area which knows no borders and which lends itself to European development.

Arts education is also a very valuable means of education, as shown, for example, by the MUS‐E project run by the Menuhin Foundation and supported by the Commission. The introduction of music lessons from primary school level has resulted in a reduction of violence in the neighbourhoods.

Question No 68 by Heidi Hautala (H−0019/97)

Subject: State of the EU's forestry industry

The European Parliament has recently debated the EU's forestry strategy in connection with the Thomas report. In its opinion appended to that report, the Committee on Economic and Monetary Affairs and Industrial Policy asks the Commission 'to present to the European Parliament a communication on forestry industry which examines the current state and competitiveness of this important industry based on the criteria of sustainable forest management'

Answer

In replying to the honourable Member's question, a distinction is necessary between forest resources and the down‐stream (forest‐based) industries related to them, since up till now these have to a large degree, been dealt with by different, but linked Community policies, respectively Agriculture and Industrial Policy, taking into consideration environmental concerns, particularly since the Rio Conference.

This situation has reflected the traditional status quo, prior to the most recent enlargement. However, as the Commission is well aware, that event radically altered affairs, such that forest resources doubled (in area), timber production increased by half, pulp production increased three‐fold and paper and board production by over 50 %.

Whilst there were no radical changes in the trade and consumption patterns of forest products as a result of the enlargement, the Community has nonetheless become a major producer and consumer of these items, the World's second after the USA. Thus, as sought by the Thomas report in general and by the Committee's opinion in particular, it is a suitable time for the Commission to review its relevant policies affecting this sector. Accordingly, for forest resources, it is foreseen to review the existing provisions in the light of experience so far and the changed circumstances following enlargement.

For the forest‐based industries, the Commission has already taken action by establishing a special sectorial unit for the Wood & Paper Industries (including graphics) in DG III. In its work programme for 1997, the unit is undertaking various analyses and co‐operation activities with the Community (and other) forest‐based industries, in order to better determine the Community competitive position and potential in this field. Following their completion, the Commission would be able to produce a communication on the forest‐based industries.

In the meantime the Commission is contributing fully to the preparation of the Community position at the InterGovernmental Panel on forests, whose 4th inter alia SFM (Sustainable Forest Management), and trade and environment aspects of forest products such as eco‐certification and the possibility of a legally‐binding instrument to encompass all economic, social and environmental aspects of forests.

***

Question No 69 by Glenys Kinnock (H−0024/97)

Subject: Child seats and airbag safety

In view of the growing concern about the risks of serious injury or death to children in rear‐facing child restraints used in the front seats of cars equipped with passenger‐side airbags, and the tragic evidence of such accidents occurring in the United States, will the Commission take the lead and encourage car manufacturers, consumer and motoring organizations to launch a major European‐wide public information campaign to inform parents of the danger of combining child seats with airbags?

Answer

The Commission is well aware of the serious risks for children transported in rear‐facing child restraints when such devices are fitted on front seats equipped with airbags. Therefore, the Commission has adopted on 17 June 1996 a directive which obliges car manufacturers to affix a warning label to such seating positions in all new cars offered for sale after 1 January 1997.

With such a regulatory requirement the Commission deems to have efficiently covered what can be done on the cars themselves.

Of course, consumer and motoring organisations are free to launch complementary information campaigns to make parents understand the dangers of fitting child seats to car seats with airbags.

Question No 70 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0048/97)

Subject: Production and marketing of bogus pharmaceutical products in the European Union

Is the Commission aware of information concerning the existence of illegal laboratories producing bogus drugs which, according to press reports, are principally active in Spain, Italy and Greece and which market their products principally in the Third World, but also in Europe? What measures is the Commission taking to smash these illegal rings so as to safeguard public health and to protect the pharmaceuticals industry from competition from the illegal production and marketing of bogus pharmaceutical products?

Answer

Production and putting on the market of medicinal products in the Member States are subject to strict Community and national rules and controls. The aim of these measures being in particular the protection of public health and the defense of legitimate commercial interests.

The European Commission does not dispose of any particular information – except for a widely worded article in the daily newspaper ‚Le Monde’ of 26 October 1996 – concerning recent cases of production or placing on the market of counterfeit medicinal products. Following the newspaper article cited above and the oral question on this issue, the European Commission is, however, planning to attract the attention of Member States to possible illegal activities in this field in upcoming pharmaceutical committee meetings.

Question No 71 by Ludivina García Arias (H−0049/97)

Subject: Directive on the transmission of television signals (situation in Spain)

In the newspapers ABC and El Mundo, the Spanish Government has announced plans regarding decoders without consulting either the industry concerned or private television stations.

Does the Commissioner responsible for industrial policy not consider that this is contrary to the provisions of Directive 95/47/EC(6) on the use of standards for the transmission of television signals?

Does the Commission not consider that this initiative runs counter to the principles of free competition in Europe?

Question No 72 by Joan Colom i Naval (H−0079/97)

Subject: The standardization of decoders

Directive 95/47/EC(7) regulates the standardization of decoders in the EU. After expiry of the deadline set in Article 8 thereof for its transposition into national legislation, the Spanish Government adopted Royal Decree‐Law 1/97 of 31 January 1997 (Official State Bulletin of 1 February 1997) which is supposed to regulate, inter alia, this standardization in Spain.

In this context, can the Commission say:

what the legal effect of this provision adopted after the deadline set in the Directive is?

whether the Royal Decree‐Law complies with Community legislation as regards both the standardization of decoders and respect for free competition?

Joint Answer

Directive 95/47/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council on the use of standards for the transmission of television signals was adopted unanimously, on 24 October 1995, by the Council, having received a large majority in the European Parliament.

The directive requires the use of standardised transmission systems for digital television.

In relation to conditional access to digital Pay‐TV service the directive also requires:

The incorporation of a descrambler conforming to the common European scrambling system in all relevant consumer equipment;

and that conditional access providers offer their services to broadcasters on fair, reasonable and nondiscriminatory terms.

The directive sets out the general principles which must be followed by Member States into national law, where the necessary detailed provisions should be set out. The directive requires that such transposition be carried out before 23 August 1996.

Question No 73 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0052/97)

Subject: Manufacturers' liability for car exhaust systems

In a case currently before the European Court of Justice, the Commission has supported VAG Svenska AB, a car importer which wants to see Swedish requirements for manufacturers to accept liability for car exhaust systems to be scrapped. The manufacturers' liability scheme was introduced on environmental grounds, and its scrapping would weaken controls on emissions from traffic. What is more, the Commission has proposed that a similar scheme be introduced at EU level. There are currently no common rules on manufacturers' liability in the EU. The Commission's actions are therefore not only hostile to the environment but also a trifle inconsistent.

How does the EU reconcile its call for a lowering of Swedish environmental standards with its own international commitments to reducing emissions into the atmosphere? Does the Commission consider that Sweden could invoke Article 100a(4) of the Treaty in order to maintain its own rules?

Answer

The European Commission strongly supports measures intending to reduce air pollution by car emissions. But the Commission also has to take care of the unity and functioning of the single market. This does not mean that the single market and economic considerations predominate environmental aspects. It means that Member States, which want to proceed in the protection of the environment have to promote European initiatives with a view to strengthening existing European legislation.

With regard to the issue of in‐service testing of vehicles, the Commission has, as the honourable Member points out in his oral question, recently put forward a proposed revision to Directive 70/220/EEC which includes harmonized provisions for in‐service testing and possible recall procedures. The Commission proposal would establish a Community‐wide coordinated and integrated system for in‐service testing which would at the same time improve the emission performance of vehicles while avoiding the proliferation of national initiatives, which risk to distort trade.

It should be noted that the system of recall in the Commission's proposal is in accordance with existing EC law in the area of whole vehicle type‐approval (Directive 70/156/EEC as amended by Directive 92/53/EEC) what cannot be said of the system practised in Sweden.

It is not possible for the Swedish Government in this case to invoke paragraph 4 of Article 100 A of the EC Treaty after Sweden has become a member of the Community as the requirements of this provision are not being fulfilled. Exemptions to the directives in question could have only been obtained during the accession negotiations, which was not the case.

***

Question No 74 by Per Gahrton (H−0084/97)

Subject: The European defence industry

The meeting of the Committee on Foreign Affairs on 19 September 1996 was attended by Commissioners Bangemann and Van den Broek. At that meeting Gary Titley's working document on the Commission communication on the challenges facing the European defence‐related industry (COM(96)0010) was discussed. Directly after the meeting I sent an open letter to the Commissioners to which no reply has yet been received. I should therefore like answers to a number of the questions I put in that letter.

Why is the Commission disregarding fundamental market economic principles where the defence industries are concerned, while other industries are allowed to go under regardless of unemployment or social problems?

Why is the Commission not seeking global cooperation for disarmament and arms export control but instead seeking to become as successful an arms dealer as the USA?

Answer

I. The defence industry is not very different from other industrial branches. That is why, in its communication of January 1996, the Commission gave priority to measures based on existing Community instruments for the single market.

However, there are certain special features in the defence industry. The following are some examples:

‐ The defence industry is a strategic branch.

‐ Governments are its only clients and in many cases they are also the main owners of defence undertakings.

‐ This branch has an enormous long‐term technological value. Some specifically defence‐oriented technologies can have both military and civilian applications.

‐ All exports of defence technology are subject to government authorization.

Moreover, late last year the POLARM group (inter‐disciplinary ad hoc working party of the Council on ‚arms policy’) adopted a document on the special features of the defence industry.

II. In its analysis of the defence industry the Commission emphasized its desire for increased competition and for this sector to be more market‐oriented (taking account of the above special features). This was because of the need not to lose any further ground in the competition with the US defence industry. That is particularly important for three reasons:

‐ Major technological and industrial bases are at stake, which can also be used for civilian purposes (dual purpose); they also involve a large number of jobs in the EU.

‐ There is a risk of a political imbalance between Europe and the USA.

‐ If the EU does not have a competitive defence technology and the related industrial basis, it will find it hard successfully to develop a European identity in the field of security and defence.

III. With regard to disarmament and export controls, the European Commission is not looking towards increasing exports of armaments. The Commission takes the view that a more efficient European defence with a larger number of common projects and a less fragmented market will be far less dependent on arms exports. Moreover, it seems unhealthy in terms of the international political situation for the USA to have a monopoly of advanced weapons systems.

A few examples of EU activities in the field of disarmament and exports:

In June 1991 the European Council adopted a declaration on the non‐proliferation and non‐export of weapons. It was based on eight criteria (see annex) for arms export policy.

In 1995 the EU adopted a joint measure to resolve the anti‐personnel mines (APM) problem. Among others, this measure included a ban on exports of mines from the Union.

***

Question No 75 by Elly Plooij‐van Gorsel (H−0087/97)

Subject: OBD systems in motor cars and their consequences for the motor industry

The proposal for a European Parliament and Council directive relating to the quality of petrol and diesel fuels and amending Council Directive 93/12/EEC(8) , and for a Directive relating to measures to be taken against air pollution by emissions from motor vehicles and amending Council Directives 70/156/EEC(9) and 70/220/EEC(10) , cover the application of a new in‐car technology for permanent emission control known as on‐board diagnostics (OBD).

Will this OBD system, and the information necessary for its repair, be made accessible to all car repairers, i.e. irrespective of whether or not they are official dealers?

If not, how does the Commission propose to guarantee competition on the internal market?

Answer

The Commission fully recognises that access should be given to enable the qualified personnel of franchised dealers, non‐franchised garages and roadside organisations to diagnose faults and repair vehicles that are equipped with on‐board diagnostic (OBD) systems. This is essential to fully utilise the benefits of OBD such that faulty vehicles are repaired as effectively as possible and pollutant emissions are kept under control.

OBD systems, the codes that indicate faults and the scan tools used to interrogate OBD systems will be based on standards published by the International Standards Organisation (ISO) and the Society of Automotive Engineers (SAE). These standards are referenced in the proposals by the Commission.

While OBD will indicate that a component is faulty, a repairer will still need information (e.g. service manuals) to show where that component is installed on the vehicle and what steps to take to repair or replace it. The conditions of the Block Exemption Regulation on selective distribution requires vehicle manufacturers to make available the information necessary to repair vehicles to parties outside their normal distribution network.

The Commission is actively considering the suitability of these regulations and the format of any additional legislation that may be necessary to ensure that such OBD repair information is commercially available in the market place to the appropriate interested parties, thereby preserving the competitive nature of the vehicle repair business in line with the internal market.
