Question No 76 by Niall Andrews (H−0096/97)

Subject: Banning 'bull bars' on motor vehicles

Will the Commission consider banning the use of 'bull bars' on motor vehicles, because they are frequently responsible for more severe accidents to pedestrians, particularly young children, than would be the case if 'bull bars' had not been fitted? Has the Commission examined this problem and if not, will it now do so with a view to presenting proposals for controlling the use of 'bull bars' at the earliest opportunity?

Answer

The Commission is, at present, considering how to solve the problems of safety, caused by the use of ‚bull bars’ on passenger cars when striking pedestrians and other unprotected road users in accidents, through technical requirements relating to the aggressivity of car fronts. Such requirements would be laid down in a new directive to be adopted by the European Parliament and the Council.

This new directive would be based on the test specifications and performance requirements established by the European Experimental Vehicle Committee which represents the leading European research laboratories in the road safety field.

On the basis of the results of a current study aimed at providing an objective opinion on the cost‐benefit aspect of such a regulation, the Commission will take up the already existing draft for the Directive concerned and finalise it in the appropriate manner.

The Commission hopes that this work can be completed before the end of this year so that the proposal can be discussed in the Council and the Parliament from the beginning of 1998.

In the meantime there is scope within national competence for Member States to take action against the use of ‚bull bars’ which are not part of the original equipment of cars and, hence, not covered by type‐approval. This is, to the Commission's information, the case of the overwhelming majority of such devices which can currently be found on vehicles in service.

The Commission assumes that the honourable Member is referring to the 90 billion pesetas balance of the 180 billion pesetas aids notified by the Spanish authorities that has still to be approved by the Commission, 89.103 billion pesetas in the form of previously approved unpaid loss compensation aid plus interest having been approved by the Commission in December 1995.

This investigation extends another investigation procedure already opened in respect of proposed future tax credits of 48 billion pesetas.

Question No 79 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H−1037/96)

Subject: Initiative on inter‐regional cooperation to combat forest fires in the Mediterranean Member States

Forest fires are a real scourge for the Mediterranean Member States, especially during the summer, and cause considerable damage, mainly to the environment but also to other sectors of their already weak economies. That being so, what is the Commission's attitude towards the idea of launching an initiative to tackle such fires – an initiative based on cooperation between the various regions of the Mediterranean and including measures such as exchanges of information and know‐how, information and awareness campaigns, common fire‐prevention programmes, etc.? How could the funding for such an initiative be found?

Answer

As the Honourable Member points out, fires can cause major damage in almost one half of Europe's wooded areas, and the Commission is very much aware of this problem.

Community action to protect forests against fires – the extension of which was approved by Parliament at the January part‐session ‐makes it possible to finance preventive measures implemented in the framework of coherent protection programmes. These measures include information and awareness campaigns, together with the carrying out of pilot projects concerning new methods and technologies designed to make the action more effective. This action has also made it possible to set up a Community information system on forest fires which is an excellent tool for evaluation and the exchange of information. These measures are funded by means of agricultural loans independently of the EAGGF.

The Commission also reminds the Honourable Member that forestry actions are included in the various planning documents approved under Objectives 1 and 5b in many Mediterranean regions. These actions include fire prevention, the protection and development of forests, and technical assistance.

Question No 80 by Robin Teverson (H−1042/96)

Subject: 'Euro‐info' publication

In the Commission's own words, SME's are urged to 'play their considerable role on the European market'. To this end, DG XXIII publishes the 'EURO‐info' bulletin which, amongst other things, keeps SME's abreast of upcoming conferences, workshops, etc. However, businesses in my constituency are informing me that the publication is consistently out‐of‐date simply because it never arrives in time. The situation has not improved, despite letters to the Commissioner bringing this to his attention. What steps does the Commission intend to take to rectify this situation which is only serving to frustrate SMEs instead of encouraging them to take part in European‐wide events to further inter‐SME participation?

Answer

The Commission thanks the Honourable Member for his interest, particularly as regards the EURO INFO bulletin.

The Commission has already taken every possible step to rectify the situation which has arisen. Having noted that the main cause of the delays in publication could be traced back to the services responsible for dispatching/distributing EURO INFO, the Commission has already signed an agreement with a new distributor, the Office for Official Publications of the European Communities (OPOCE).

Under the terms of this contract which will enter into force on 1 April 1997, all copies of EURO INFO (roughly 60, 000) will have to be distributed within three days of the date on which the OPOCE receives the documents (compared to two or three weeks at present). It should also be stressed that since September 1996 EURO INFO has been available on the Internet in all the Community languages except Greek before the end of the month to which the information it contains relates.

Finally, a detailed questionnaire appeared or will appear in the December 1996 and January and February 1997 editions. This questionnaire will not only enable the publishers to update the list of subscribers, but above all to tailor the contents of EURO INFO more effectively to the needs of SMEs.

The various measures outlined above will already help to bring about an appreciable improvement in the quality of the information supplied to SMEs. The Commission will continue to monitor the situation very closely. Should the improvements fall short of expectations, other solutions might be considered.

Question No 81 by Alan Gillis (H−1044/96)

Subject: Civil war in Sudan

Given the ongoing civil war in Sudan, what steps is the Commission prepared to take to ensure the installation of a monitoring procedure in order to bring this tragedy to an end and to ensure adequate humanitarian aid to the people of Sudan who are the victims in this conflict?

Answer

The European Commission is following the situation in the Sudan very closely. Development aid was suspended in March 1990 with the agreement of the Member States due to the prevailing lack of human rights, lack of both democracy or moves towards it and a lack of meaningful efforts to negotiate a peaceful end to the civil war in the south of the country. All these issues remain on the agenda of the ‚frank and crucial dialogue’ the Community initiated with the Sudan in order to bring over to the Sudan its concern in these matters and to help overcome the problems with a view to work towards normalization of the relations between the Community and the Sudan. Despite these efforts, the Sudan has not responded positively, and the problems raised have not been addressed by the government in Khartoum. Thus, development aid remains suspended, though humanitarian aid still continues, principally to the population affected by the civil war in the south of the country.

Meanwhile, the Government of the Sudan maintains that it is above reproach. This view is not shared by the international community, but it does make it extremely difficult to see how the Sudan would agree to have independent monitors.

Then there is the question of guaranteeing an acceptable level of security for monitors. This would be almost impossible in the present circumstances.

As the war continued, the displaced and war‐affected population were – and still are – in dire need of substantial humanitarian relief. The European Community has been able to rise to the challenge and has, since 1994, provided humanitarian aid amounting to over ECU 61m through the European Community Humanitarian Office (ECHO) together with some ECU 35.2 of food aid and is still continuing to provide such aid.

Question No 82 by Arlene Mccarthy (H−1047/96)

Subject: Value added tax on the transportation of human remains within the EU

Given that, in the single European market, there are inevitably an increasing number of cases of citizens moving away from the place where they would eventually like to be buried or cremated, does the Commission not feel that it is both callous and inappropriate that a bereaved family, under the EC Sixth Directive, should be charged VAT on the transportation of a relative's remains, a process surely expensive enough without this additional cost?

Answer

Value added tax is a general tax which in principle must be levied on all goods and services. This broad application guarantees the neutrality of the tax in the various sectors of activity. The death of a person is always a very sad occasion, but nevertheless his or her burial often creates a need for the services offered by a commercial sector, that of funeral directors.

Although some Member States exempt this activity from VAT on the basis of a temporary derogation, the principle of its taxation is laid down in Community law, which does, however, authorize the imposition of a lower rate of VAT.

The transport of corpses generally forms part of the service offered by funeral directors and is covered by the relevant tax arrangements. If, however, the transport takes place separately, it is subject to the general rules governing transport, i.e. it is taxed in full in the Member State.

The Commission does not plan to propose any changes to the current system. In particular, it does not plan to exempt the transport of corpses from VAT. Such a measure would deprive the transporting firm of its right to deduct the tax for this type of service, thereby complicating the administration of its activities.

Question No 83 by Mark Killilea (H−1049/96)

Subject: Continued payment of headage payments

Could the Commission inform me if a statement recently made by the Irish Department of Agriculture is true, i.e. that it was desired by the Commission to change the headage payment system, because, claimed the Government, the Commission's attitude was that the funds allocated to this measure represented too high a percentage of the overall allocation to the Structural Improvement and Rural Development element of the Operation Programme?

If that be the truth and as those payments are being made to disadvantaged areas, the weakest agricultural sections in our country, what proposals is the Commission suggesting that the Government should implement as an alternative, understanding as we do that the headage payments were a limited support and a very good one at that?

Answer

The major role played by compensatory allowances in helping maintain farm incomes in the less‐favoured areas is expressly recognised in the Community support framework for Ireland and the operational programme for agriculture, rural development and forestry 1994‐1999. However, there must be a proper balance between this measure and others in the operational programme to ensure that farming can become more self‐reliant and integrated into the rural economy. According to a commitment in the Community support framework, a special examination of the system of compensatory allowances with a view to amending and better targeting the measure was carried out. Subsequently, the opinion of the Monitoring Committee was requested in September 1996 on proposals which would make some economies in the operation of the measure. The Commission is currently discussing this matter with the Irish authorities.

Question No 84 by Bryan Cassidy (H−1052/96)

Subject: Economic impact of Commission proposals

In Official Journal C 359 of 28 November 1996, DG XXIV issued an open invitation to tender for a 'Study on the economic impact of the directive proposal on the sale and guarantees of consumer goods' (96/C 359/19).

In Official Journal C 361 of 30 November 1996, DG XXIV issued another open invitation to tender for a 'Study on the economic impact on small and medium‐sized retail businesses of the implementation of the European Parliament and Council Directive on consumer protection in the indication of the prices of products offered to consumers' (96/C 361/09).

Why did the Commission not carry out these studies before adopting the two draft directives?

Answer

The study on the economic impact on small and medium‐sized retail businesses of the implementation of the European Parliament and Council Directive on Consumer protection in the indication of products offered to consumers was commissioned as a reaction to amendment 5, adopted by the European Parliament in the first reading of the proposal for a directive in April 1996. This amendment asks the Commission, before the Directive enters into force, to draw up, an accurate statement of the cost of its full implementation to retail businesses's especially in the small and medium‐sized undertaking sector.

Furthermore this study will prepare the report on the application of the provision in the draft Directive concerning the small retail business (amendment 22, adopted by the European Parliament in the first reading, and Article 13 of the Council's common position).

Therefore this study responds to a request by the Council and especially the European Parliament.

The study on the economic impact of the directive proposal on the sale and guarantees of consumer goods is intended to broaden the considerations of the Commission in this field. The Commission's assessment of the economic impact had been criticised after the presentation of the proposal. This assessment was based on the fact that the gains in consumers satisfaction would outweigh the costs. The study has ben commissioned to confirm these considerations.

Question No 85 by Lone Dybkjær (H−1053/96)

Subject: Environmental considerations and public procurement

Public procurement accounts for a very large proportion of overall consumption. It is therefore essential for the environment that environmental considerations be given priority in public procurement transactions. This will not only benefit the environment in specific cases but will also help to set higher standards for the market generally.

Does the Commission not take the view that it would be appropriate to improve the legal situation in this sector by introducing clearer rules on the right to impose environmental requirements?

Has it thought how the scope for environmental requirements might be extended to include the environmental assessment of a product's entire life cycle?

Answer

The Commission recognizes the importance of environmental protection policy and the need, in accordance with Article 130 R of the Treaty, to take full account of environmental protection requirements when drawing up and implementing other Community policy.

Last November the Commission adopted a Green Paper entitled ‚Public procurement in the European Union: areas of study for the future’. This was intended to provide a framework for a wide‐ranging debate on public procurement policy and was addressed, among others, to the European Parliament. The Green Paper provides a detailed description of what current directives on public contracts offer in terms of environmental protection.

For example, the directives make it possible to exclude any entrepreneurs guilty of an offence or serious misconduct resulting from the violation of legislation designed to promote environmental interests; to make the performance of contracts awarded conditional upon the respect of environmental obligations; to include environmental aspects in the technical conditions governing the characteristics of the works, supplies or services which are the subject of contracts. In this respect, the purchasing bodies can encourage companies to adopt an active environmental approach by not rejecting offers of products containing reconditioned parts or recycled materials, when the technical performances of these products meet the requirements laid down in the general conditions. Subject to certain conditions, the directives also make it possible to include the environmental protection objective in the selection criteria for contract candidates and in the criteria for awarding contracts, in order to identify the most economically beneficial tender.

After analysing the scope provided by the directives, the Commission asks the recipients of the Green Paper if they consider them to be sufficient or if other measures must be taken. In the latter case, the Commission would be prepared to make the necessary proposals in order to ensure better environmental protection when applying Community rules governing public procurement, while at the same time ensuring that competition is protected.

The Commission is trying to develop dialogue between the social partners at European level. But it does not intervene at national level in this area.

Question No 88 by Concepció Ferrer (H−0059/97)

Subject: Humanitarian aid to the former Yugoslavia

In its report on humanitarian aid to the former Yugoslavia (A4‐0174/96)(11) , the European Parliament stressed the need to provide funding for peace education programmes to help foster tolerance among peoples.

Has the Commission adopted any such programme? Does it have plans to carry out joint actions with other international organizations in this or any other sphere?

Answer

In implementing its programmes of humanitarian aid for former Yugoslavia, the Commission has given the broadest possible interpretation to the possibilities offered by the Humanitarian Aid Regulation. This is in line with the resolution of the European Parliament on Humanitarian Aid to Former Yugoslavia. In this spirit the Commission financed programmes covering not only traditional humanitarian aid (food, medical supplies and shelter), but also restoration of basic services and psycho‐social assistance.

At this time the psycho‐social programmes carried out not only in Bosnia and Herzegovina but also in Croatia and the Federal Republic of Yugoslavia, are rather better designed to relieve immediate post war traumas than to start a programme of active social integration. Nevertheless, at least in one programme in Bosnia and Herzegovina, implemented through Children's Aid Direct specific provision is made for teachers training in the hope that their work may then act as a catalyst for the younger generations to understand the need to restore a genuine multiethnic society.

The Commission is also working closely with UNHCR and the High Representative to create enhanced conditions favouring the return of refugees. In doing so, it is examining the possibility of using such bodies as the Bosnian Women's Initiative. This grouping while newly established, does provide a model showing how different ethnic groupings can work towards common objectives.

Question No 89 by Thomas Megahy (H−0006/97)

Subject: Age limits in recruitment to EU institutions: advertisements in Ireland

What arrangements is the Commission making for advertising its recruitment competitions in Ireland after the adoption of the Employment Equality Bill 1996, which prohibits age discrimination in published employment advertisements?

Answer

The Staff Regulations of the Community Institutions, which permit the use of age limits in the competitions, are an autonomous regime which is applied in the Commission's administration and recruitment.

When recruiting officials from 15 Member States, the European Commission needs to take particular aspects into account. The use of age limits has been one tool in managing a high number of applicants. To illustrate the management problems it can be noted that in the latest competitions for all the Member States, there have been 50, 000 applicants, of which 600 are chosen to the reserve lists.

In the recruitment of officials from the new Member States, the Commission has organised two kinds of competitions. Firstly, competitions for the lowest grades have been addressed to the young officials who are beginning their career. Secondly, there have been competitions for the senior officials who h ave more competence and working experience and accordingly, a higher age limit has applied.

It is equally important to note that the Commission has recently decided within its new policy on temporary agents to organise competitions also for the specialists, senior officials.

The competitions of the new Member States, where women participate to a great deal in working life, have proved that the age limits have not prevented women to apply. Furthermore, the results have shown that an age is an important factor to mobility. At the lowest grades, 51 % of the recruited officials were women, whereas, at the middle management grades, the correspondent figure was 41 %.

In addition, the Commission has set annual objectives for female recruitment which has also given positive results.

Question No 90 by Kenneth Coates (H−0009/97)

Subject: Unemployed citizens: freedom of movement

Preparations are under way for a number of marches by unemployed citizens to converge on Amsterdam for the Intergovernmental Conference. Many unemployed people would like to participate and show their concerns about social exclusion. However, in some countries such action may lead to the loss of their benefits. In such cases, the loss of employment will imply the loss of civil rights as well. What action does the Commission propose to uphold the rights of every citizen to lobby the European institutions and influence social policy?

Answer

The Community provisions on the coordination of the national social security schemes for workers moving within the Community (Regulation 1408/71) guarantee that unemployed people can retain their unemployment benefits for a period of three months if they go to seek work in another Member State and register with the employment services in that Member State.

There is no Community provision on the retention of unemployment benefits when an unemployed person moves within the Community for other reasons – such as family visits, holidays or to take parts in events. In these cases the retention of benefits depends solely on the provision in the national legislation.

In so far as influencing social policy at Community level is concerned, the Commission is anxious to involve citizens – and organizations close to the citizen – in the policy‐making process in all feasible ways. The 1994 White Paper on social policy was produced on the basis of a consultative process open to citizens.

The Commission intends that 1997 should see a strengthening of the dialogue between the Community Institutions and the non‐governmental organisations concerned with social policy at Community level. The new budget line (B3‐4101) to aid charitable associations in the spirit of Declaration 23 attached to the EC Treaty will be deployed in support of this objective.

Question No 91 by Freddy Blak (H−0010/97)

Subject: EU aid for tobacco production

The Danish anti‐smoking campaign has just established that smoking is the most frequent cause of death in Danish society, causing more deaths each year than road accidents and drowning, AIDS, alcohol, drugs, suicide and murder. The EU actively supports European tobacco production and thus contributes to the spread of diseases such as lung cancer, thrombosis, cerebral haemorrhages, osteoporosis and arteriosclerosis. What political objectives justify such consequences? When will the Commission rectify the disparity between its health policy and its agricultural policy? Does it consider that such paradoxes are good for the EU's reputation?

Answer

The Commission considers tobacco control to be a key Community policy area and this is reflected in the priorities for action under the Third Cancer Action Programme which was adopted by this House and the Council on 29 March last year. Moreover, in December 1996 the Commission adopted a Communication on tobacco control policy outlining measures adopted at Community level to date to combat smoking, and suggesting further action which could be taken at Community level in the future. The purpose of this Communication is to promote a wideranging debate on the policy to be followed in reducing smoking.

On the same occasion, the Commission adopted a Report into the organisation of the tobacco market which proposes further reforms to the tobacco sector, following on from those made in 1992. Changes proposed include increasing from 1 % to 2 % the levy on producers' subsidies. The sums raised would be put towards the Community Fund for Research and Information on Tobacco, to be used to finance both projects on the dangers of smoking, and research into crop conversion and less damaging varieties of tobacco.

The adoption in parallel of a Communication on smoking prevention, and the report on reforms to the raw tobacco market illustrates the Commission's will to reduce the disparities to which the Honourable Member refers.

Question No 92 by José Girão Pereira (H−0011/97)

Subject: Sanctions for failure to implement the Third Multiannual Guidance Programme

At a time when the Fourth Multiannual Guidance Programme is about to enter into force, certain Member States have not yet met the objectives set in the Third MGP and have even in some cases increased their fleet capacity.

How does the Commission view this state of affairs?

Does the Commission intend to apply sanctions? If so, what kind of sanctions?

Answer

The final results of the Third Multiannual Guidance Programmes, or MGP III, will not be known in detail until the end of April 1997.

1. ‐ Member States which have not met the targets set by their programme must, pursuant to the provisions of Article 10 of Regulation 3699/93, suspend any system of public aid to fleet modernization leading to increased fishing.

2. ‐ The Commission can also introduce an infringement procedure under Article 169 of the Treaty for failure to respect Community regulations.

3. ‐ Finally, for the future, the Commission's position is clear and well known: the general fishing reductions decided under MGP IV must be applied to the targets laid down under MGP III. This method of calculation carries over to MGP IV the share of reductions not achieved under MGP III and can be considered as an additional sanction to the two previous ones.

On the other hand, Member States which implement measures which go beyond the targets laid down in MGP III will receive a target credit at the start of MGP IV.

Question No 93 by Robert Evans (H−0013/97)

Subject: Children's books and images of ethnic minorities

Is the Commission aware of accusations that British publishers are deliberately excluding images of those from ethnic minorities from their books in order to sell them abroad – including in other Member States (The Observer of 27 October 1996)?

Would the Commission not agree that this, if found to be of substance, is a shocking and unacceptable practice, that cannot be tolerated?

In this, the European Year Against Racism, what action is the Commission willing to take to investigate and combat such offensive acts which should have no place in our multicultural European society?

Answer

The Commission is not aware of the allegations raised by the Honourable Parliamentarian, but it considers such practices, if found to be of substance, to be quite unwarranted.

The Commission has no powers to investigate such practices. It has, however, recently proposed the creation of a European Monitoring Centre for racism and xenophobia(12) , with a mandate to supply the institutions of the European Community and the Member States with objective, reliable and comparative data on racism, xenophobia and anti‐Semitism. Moreover, during the European Year against Racism a particular emphasis is being placed on highlighting the positive contribution that ethnic minorities make to all Member States.

Question No 94 by Luciano Vecchi (H−0016/97)

Subject: European Senior Citizens' Parliament

In 1993 a 'European Senior Citizens' Parliament' met in Luxembourg, and the venture, organized on the initiative of the European Parliament, proved to be a great success.

It has been proposed that the experience be repeated in 1998, on the eve of UN 'International Year of the Elderly'.

Would the Commission be willing to give political and financial support to such a move?

Does the Commission consider that the European Senior Citizens' Parliament could be a useful means both of encouraging old people to involve themselves in European politics and of raising awareness within the Community institutions of issues concerning the elderly?

Answer

The Commission warmly welcomed the earlier Senior Parliament and will do the same again if the Parliament chooses to go ahead with the current proposal. The message coming from these and similar events – that society should listen to the voice of the older generation – is fully in line with the Commission's efforts in this area in recent years. The 1993 European Senior Parliament was successful in that the core message of participation by older people did get transmitted clearly – to the media and to concerned organizations across Europe.

A Senior Parliament taking place in 1998 would serve as a bridgehead for European participation with regard to the United Nations year for older people in 1999.

A new Senior Parliament will require careful planning and preparation, and the Commission is prepared to be involved at these stages if this should be requested by the Parliament. With regard to the question of financial support, the expenditure incurred by the 1993 European Senior Parliament was provided out of the Parliament's own budget. Therefore, the financial aspects of a new event needs to be carefully assessed by the Parliament itself.

Question No 95 by Philippe Monfils (H−0017/97)

Subject: Relocation of a Belgian firm from the Walloon region to Ireland

Boston Scientific, a surgical equipment manufacturer, has recently decided to close its factory in Battice in Belgium and transfer its production centre to Ireland, leading to the loss of 230 jobs in the Walloon Region, for the simple reason that it will obtain a significant level of European aid, since Ireland is an Objective 1 region.

Given that the philosophy behind regional aid is the convergence of the economies of the various regions and the growth of employment, does not the Commission consider that, in terms of economic ethics, there is a misuse of European subsidies when a company relocates – with disastrous social consequences and even though the company is in no way in difficulties – solely in order to obtain more European aid without having the least impact on the total level of jobs?

Answer

In conformity with Article 130A of the EC Treaty, the Community develops and pursues actions leading to the strengthening of its economic and social cohesion. Aid from the Structural Funds is normally provided via the cofinancing of programmes, for example for industrial development. Such a programme may contain a number of aid schemes for industry. The management of all such national aid schemes lies with the competent national authorities, which select the individual projects benefiting from the scheme.

It should also be recalled that all Member States provide assistance to foreign direct investments and have schemes similar to the one in Ireland supported by the Industrial Development Agency (IDA).

The Community contributes 51.1 MECU to the IDA Scheme for fixed asset support, with a global envelope of public support for the years 1994‐1999 of 373.6 MECU. This is around 13 %. It should be emphasized that aid from the Structural Funds for industrial development must be granted on the basis of State aid schemes approved by the Commission beforehand. As for national or regional authorities, they have the option of making aid conditional on the maintenance for a certain period of jobs created.

With regard to State aids, Ireland is an eligible region under Article 92(3)(a) of the EC Treaty, which allows aid to promote the economic development of areas where the standard of living is abnormally low or where there is serious underemployment.

Studies undertaken by the European Parliament regarding the location or relocation of enterprises have shown that aid for investment is only one of many factors considered in reaching such a decision. In the Irish context, it would seem that other elements such as salary costs, tax regime and availability of skills influenced Boston Scientific's decision to increase its already existing activities in Ireland.

Question No 96 by Jan Andersson (H−0021/97)

Subject: Employment in the maritime transport sector

Tax‐free sales are currently a feature of air and maritime transport links between EU Member States and are very significant as regards employment in those sectors. A study carried out for the Swedish National Maritime Administration shows that, in Swedish waters alone, 2500 jobs would disappear if tax‐free sales ended. Certain regions would be particularly hard hit. Some 18 million EU citizens are currently unemployed. Every effort must be made to preserve existing jobs and create new ones.

In view of this, does the Commission not consider that the current tax‐free sales arrangements should continue within the European Union?

Answer

The Commission has already had occasion to stress its position regarding tax‐free intra‐Community sales. It believes that these sales are incompatible with the principles of completing the internal market.

It reminds you that the Council, together with Parliament, adopted such a position back in 1991. Finally, it stresses that the Council has, nevertheless, granted the sectors concerned a substantial period (more than 7 years) in which to adapt to the new situation, while at the same time deciding to put an end once and for all to tax‐free sales inside the European Community on 30/06/99.

The study mentioned by the Honourable Member has not been brought to the Commission's attention. It would nevertheless like to point out that the removal of the tax advantage granted to a sector of activity does not necessarily mean the end of this activity and it would be appropriate for the sector in question to employ this transitional period in order to develop a strategy which will enable it to adapt.

Question No 97 by David Morris (H−0026/97)

Subject: Common Agricultural Policy

There are areas within the EU, such as Less Favoured Areas etc, where an agricultural subsidy is necessary for employment, environmental and social reasons. However, at present the largest proportion of EU support for agriculture goes to farmer and agri‐businesses not faced with these problems.

With the increasing pressure for open competition in agricultural production, what action does the Commission intend to take to ensure that, in future, subsidies are directed to where they are needed and where they will return positive benefits?

Answer

The less‐favoured areas scheme is only one of a large range of measures in the Community agricultural and ruraldevelopment policy aiming at environmental, social and economic objectives. Thus in addition to benefiting from specific compensation for their natural handicaps, farmers in less‐favoured areas profit from other measures under Structural Funds Objective 5a for speeding up the adjustment of agricultural structures. Furthermore, these farmers may benefit from support under Objectives 1, 5b and 6 for the development of certain rural regions. The Common Market Organizations, which apply throughout the Community, have been reformed since 1992 in view of increasing competition, deregulation and globalization in agriculture.

The accompanying measures of the 1992 reforms were specifically designed to promote positive effects from farming.

In order to address future challenges to the agricultural sector and rural areas, the Commission is persisting in its efforts to better integrate, simplify and evaluate its rural‐development and market policies under the Common Agricultural Policy, and thus direct subsidies to where they are needed and where they will return positive benefits.

Question No 98 by Alex Smith (H−0029/97)

Subject: Nuclear shipments

On 13 January 1997 a ship arrived at the Scrabster port in the North of Scotland, delivering a cargo of nuclear fuel from Germany to the Dounreay nuclear plant; on the same day the 'Pacific Teal' cargo ship left the port of Cherbourg in France with a cargo of 20 tonnes of high level nuclear waste from the COGEMA plant at La Hague destined for Japan after reprocessing.

Will the Commission examine both the prior notification regulations covering the sea shipment of nuclear materials, including radioactive wastes, and the safety provisions in place for the nuclear transports in the light of the scientific and technical criticisms contained in the report: 'The sea transport of vitrified high‐level radioactive wastes: unresolved safety issues', published by the Nuclear Control Institute in Washington D.C. in December 1996?

Answer

The safety of transport of radioactive materials, including the prior notification of shipments, is regulated by the Member States on the basis of the IAEA Safety Standards for the Safe Treatment of Radioactive Material (Safety Series No 6‐1995 edition – amended in 1990).

The modal aspects of safe transport of radioactive materials by sea are regulated by the implementation of the International Maritime Dangerous Goods Code (IMDG‐Code) and the Code of Practice for the Safe Carriage of Irradiated Nuclear Fuel, Plutonium and High Level Radioactive Waste in Flasks on Board Ships.

Specific provisions are provided for by the Council Directive 92/3/EURATOM(13) on the supervision and control of shipments of radioactive waste between Member States and into and out of the Community. The corresponding standard document was established by the Commission Decision of 1/10/93(14) . The procedure aims essentially at ensuring that shipments of radioactive waste take place only after the prior informed consent of all the States involved.

With regard to the findings, published in the NCI study of December 1996 on 'The Sea Transport of Vitrified HighLevel Radioactive Waste', it is noted that they were drawn to the attention of the IAEA, the forum in which the international transport regulations are regularly reviewed and updated in order to take stock of the newest level of knowledge in science and technology.

Question No 99 by Christine Oddy (H−0083/97)

Subject: Transportation of nuclear waste

Is the Commission aware that trains transporting nuclear waste pass through my constituency and that in January, in clear contravention of safety rules, the train was parked immediately next to homes and factories for 5 hours to allow staff to investigate a problem on one of the flasks containing radioactive waste? As this is one of three such incidents involving this train in recent years, what steps will the Commission take to press for greater security and safety measures in transporting radioactive waste?

Answer

The event mentioned by the honourable Member of Parliament is related to the shipment of irradiated fuel on 4 January 1997 from the Dungeness B power station to Sellafield in Cumbria.

During a stop at Rugby for inspection of the three wagons carrying the irradiated nuclear fuel casks it was discovered that their weather covers were not properly padlocked. These covers are not intended to provide either safety or security and their use is optional under the power stations operating procedures. The covers serve to keep the weather off the fuel flasks, thus obviating the need to clean the flasks prior to unloading.

The event has received the necessary interest but according to the Commission's information has not revealed any radiological consequences since the radiation level did not exceed the normal, low levels associated with fuel flasks. Nevertheless the power stations operators were asked by the competent authorities to take the necessary remedial measures to avoid reoccurrence.

On a regular basis during the meetings of the Commission's Standing Working Group of national experts for the transport of radioactive material, events in the Member States are discussed to share information and learn lessons from transport practices in order to verify the adequacy of the transport regulations and the way these are implemented.

Question No 100 by Hugh Mcmahon (H−0035/97)

Subject: Working time directive – excluded sectors

In the original working time directive 93/104/EC(15) transport was one of the original excluded sectors; however, the Commission has, on several occasions, faithfully promised to introduce legislation in this area.

Will the Commissioner inform Parliament of the outcome of his discussions with the social partners and with Commissioner Flynn on the excluded sectors?

Answer

As a first step, the Commission requested each of the Joint Committees in the excluded sectors concerned to provide joint descriptions of the existing situation with regard to working time arrangements in their sector and to give their recommendations as to how, taking account of the specific nature of the work in the sector, the principles of the Working Time Directive might be applied. The current situation following this request may be summarised as follows.

In the Joint Committee on Rail Transport, agreement has been reached that the provisions of the Directive should be extended to all workers in the industry, subject to the condition that similar provisions be applied to other transport sectors at the same time.

In air transport, there is agreement that the provisions of the Directive should similarly be extended to cover all ground staff (some 80 % of personnel).

Within the sea transport sector, the International Labour Organization adopted Convention No 180 on seafarers' hours of work and the manning of ships in October 1996. This successful outcome was achieved largely as a result of the tireless efforts of both employer and trade union members of the Joint Committee on maritime transport, supported by the Commission.

At present, there is no comparable progress to report in respect of road transport, inland waterway transport or sea fishing. Furthermore, the absence of Joint Committee or Social Partner structures in the other excluded sectors makes progress difficult.

The Commission is currently preparing the White Paper on the sectors and activities excluded from the Working Time Directive, the aim of which will be to explore the best ways of ensuring, with regard to working time, the protection of the health and safety of the workers concerned as well as health and safety generally, with due respect for the principles of subsidiarity and proportionality.

The White Paper will analyze the situation in respect of each of these sectors and activities and set out the Commission's views on the possible options to take this matter forward. The factual part of the White Paper will be based on the work which has been undertaken by the social partners in the Joint Sectoral Committees in each of the five transport sectors and the sea fishing sector and on the studies which the Commission arranged to be undertaken in respect of doctors in training and ‚other work at sea’. It is expected that the White Paper in question will be issued in the first half of this year.

Question No 102 by Ivar Virgin (H−0041/97)

Subject: Tougher rules on the use of meat meal in animal feedingstuffs

In Sweden, the use of meat meal in animal feedingstuffs has been severely restricted and its processing and manufacture subject to very stringent requirements since 1988. Owing to the cases of BSE discovered in Germany, similar measures have now been called for there, too. In view of this and of the considerable risks which the use of meat meal in animal feedingstuffs entails, does the Commission intend to bring in restrictions and tougher rules on the manufacture and use of meat meal?

Answer

The Commission has a three part policy with respect to meat and bonemeal use in animal feed.
1. Safe sourcing of raw material.
2. Safe processing.
3. Safe use of meat and bonemeal.

Part 3 has been in force throughout the Community since July 1994 with the prohibition on feeding mammalian protein to ruminants. The final stage of part 2 comes into force on 1 April 1997 with the compulsory use of the most vigorous rendering process for all mammalian waste material.

Several Member States have implemented part 1 on a national basis but, to date, the Commission has not been able to persuade the majority of Member States of the need for safe sourcing.

The Commission believes that these three tiers of protection would give the necessary health protection.

Question No 103 by Julio Añoveros Trias de Bes (H−0051/97)

Subject: Rules on automobile distribution and independent garages

The rules on automobile distribution give independent garages the right to know the technical specifications of automobiles in order to carry out repairs efficiently and safely. However, automobile manufacturers are supplying these specifications in software programmes that are covered by the provisions on the protection of intellectual property. What does the Commission intend to do about this practice which is contrary to the principle of freedom of movement? What steps can independent garages take to defend their interests?

Answer

Article 6, paragraph 1 of Commission Regulation 1475/95 of 28 June 1995 states that the exemption per category of automobile distribution agreements from the ban laid down in Article 85, paragraph 1 of the EC Treaty does not apply in certain cases. In particular, under No. 12 of this provision, the condition to which the Honourable Member refers states that: ’Exemption does not apply when:

12) the manufacturer refuses to make accessible, possibly against payment, to repairers who are not part of its distribution network, the necessary techniques to repair or service contractual products or equivalent products or to implement environmental protection standards, unless this information is covered by an intellectual property right or is a substantial and identified secret know‐how; in this case, the necessary technical information must not be unreasonably refused’.

By means of this provision the Commission sought to give independent garages the widest possible access to the technical information necessary for repairing motor vehicles, while taking into account the rights attached to industrial property.

The inclusion of this clause in Article 6 of the Regulation should allow the competent national courts to check that the exemption conditions provided for by the Regulation are met and, if not, to identify the possibly anti‐competitive nature of the practice in question in order to, in the given circumstances, award damages to compensate for the alleged prejudice.

If, for example, automobile manufacturers, by using software programmes protected by intellectual property rights, try to prevent independent garages from gaining access to the technical information necessary for the repair and maintenance of vehicles, the above provision applies.

Interested parties who find themselves wronged by such actions can appeal directly to the national courts which, according to the Commission, must appraise in each individual case whether or not abuse has taken place and what indemnity should be taken into consideration.

National courts can also ask the Commission for advice and elements of fact and of law useful to the procedure.

Question No 104 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H−0053/97)

Subject: Fisheries meeting in Casablanca

What will be the objectives and substance of the fisheries meeting to be held in Casablanca in May 1997 and what impact will it have on the current fisheries agreement between the European Union and Morocco?

Casablanca 97 partnership – Fishing industries is a joint initiative by the European Commission and the Moroccan Ministry of Fisheries and the Merchant Navy intended to encourage lasting partnerships between fishing sector industries in the Community and Morocco. Around one hundred Moroccan projects were selected and their particulars included in a catalogue distributed in the Member States with a view to stimulating technical, commercial and financial cooperation. Interested European companies will have the opportunity to discuss the projects with their potential Moroccan partners at contact days to be held on 26 and 27 May in Casablanca. In regard to Spain, the FROM is responsible for distributing this catalogue and identifying potential Spanish companies.

This initiative helps to effectively implement the measures introduced under Article 3 of the cooperation agreement for sea fishing between the Community and the Kingdom of Morocco.

Question No 105 by David Bowe (H−0055/97)

Subject: Civil liability and the use of biotechnological products

Does the Commission think that liberalization in the contained use of genetically modified organisms should be accompanied by a provision ensuring strict liability in case of accidental escape or the misuse of biotechnological products? If not, why not?

Answer

The proposed amendment of Directive 90/21/EEC(16) with its harmonized approach to risk assessment of activities and selection of containment measures, defined standards of containment facilities and relating administrative oversight to the risk of the work being undertaken, will better safeguard the appropriate safety standards for human health and the environment. As the Commission Proposal will result in a clarification and better definition of the containment measures to be applied to the contained use of genetically modified micro‐organisms, the safety standards applied to the contained use of genetically modified microorganisms will not be lowered. The proposed modification of Directive 90/219/EEC will not affect the provisions to be applied to biotechnological products containing genetically modified organisms, which fall under the scope of Directive 90/220/EEC(17) on the deliberate release into the environment of genetically modified organisms.

Therefore, the Commission does not perceive the need to include provisions on liability in the proposed amendment of Directive 90/219/EEC. The Commission is aware of the need to ensure that the issue of environmental liability is correctly dealt with at the Community level and decided on 29 January 1997 to prepare a White Paper on an EC liability regime.

Question No 106 by Astrid Thors (H−0058/97)

Subject: Commission plans for financing Baltic cooperation

The Commission launched its Baltic initiative in 1996, but in practice cooperation in the region is currently an extremely complex matter. For instance, co‐financing between the Interreg II C programme and the Phare and Tacis programmes is, in its present form, a very slow process, if at all possible.

Is the Commission prepared to introduce a new common financing instrument (own budget line) for the cofinancing of joint projects between Member States and Tacis and Phare countries in the Baltic region?

Answer

The Commission's Baltic Sea Region Initiative sets out how the Community supports cooperation among the Baltic Sea States. The Initiative mentions that it will be implemented with existing instruments and financial resources. The Commission is discussing priorities with the countries in the region.

The Commission has developed mechanisms to encourage joint PHARE, TACIS and INTERREG projects. The 1996 PHARE Baltic Sea Region Cross‐Border Cooperation Programme and the 1996 TACIS Cross‐Border Cooperation Programme provide funds for the partner countries' participation in INTERREG activities. The first projects are under preparation.

In cooperation with the different partners, the Commission is examining ways of overcoming practical difficulties in using PHARE, TACIS and structural funds as complementary sources. The creation of a new budget line covering support to the Baltic Sea Region would not be compatible, however, with the structure of the Community budget.

Question No 107 by Carole Tongue (H−0060/97)

Subject: Implementation of European broadcasting quotas

In accordance with Article 4(3) of the 'Television Without Frontiers' (TVWF) Directive 89/552/EEC(18) , the UK Government has submitted information to the Commission concerning the implementation of Articles 4 and 5 of the said directive. What is the Commission's view on this information, and in particular on the results of BSkyB, which is still well below the 51 % level for European‐origin broadcasting quotas on all its channels(19) , given that this broadcaster has now been in existence for well over three years and can no longer be considered a new entrant on the market? What will the Commission do to ensure that the TVWF Directive is complied with to secure a truly common legal regime for all?

Answer

Article 4 of the 1989 ‚television without frontiers’ Directive stipulates that Member States shall ensure ‚where practicable and by appropriate means’ that broadcasters reserve a majority proportion of their transmission time for European works. This wording, which is the result of a difficult compromise, departs from the Commission's initial proposal and from the wishes expressed by Parliament at that time. It introduces an element of flexibility indicating that attainment of the majority proportion objectives can be overridden by technical constraints or economic imperatives. The provisions amount to an obligation for Member States to behave in a certain manner, while allowing the discretion in choosing the means of attaining the desired result. Being unable to agree on a more binding text, the Council nevertheless wished the flexibility of the legal instrument to be counterbalanced by the monitoring role assigned to the Commission.

Since the entry into force of the Directive, the Commisssion has published two monitoring reports, covering respectively the periods 1991/1992 and 1993/1994. These show that the four broadcasters in the United Kingdom which have by far the largest audience share (90 %) transmit well in excess of the majority proportion of European works (60 % on average). Certain thematic or recently created channels failed, however, to meet the objective. Consequently, the Commission has used the means at its disposal to persuade the UK government to do its utmost to ensure, by appropriate means and where practicable, that the targets are met by all the channels to which they apply. The UK Secretary of State for National Heritage has assured the Commission that the UK government places the highest priority on ensuring that the UK meets its Community obligations and that it will not hesitate to use, if necessary, the legislative means at it s disposal. At the same time, interim information covering 1995 and early 1996 was supplied to the Commission showing that the progress was being made with respect to the channels that had failed to attain the majority proportion. The Commission is continuing to monitor the situation and expects to receive further information from the UK authorities in the framework of the 1995/1996 monitoring exercise with a view to publishing its 3rd monitoring report later this year (1997).

As regards the system itself, the Commission, while acknowledging the need for an appropriate level of flexibility, has made it clear that it has never been happy with the lack of legal security introduced by the phrase ‚where practicable’. This is why, in the framework of its 1995 proposal to amend the ‚television with frontiers’ Directive, the Commission proposed to delete this phrase and to replace, for thematic channels, the transmission time obligations with investment obligation, the latter being better adapted to the nature of such channels. The Council, however, in its Common Position, decided to retain the 1989 provisions. The European Parliament did not amend the Council's position in its second reading last November. The Commission will therefore have to continue to implement the 1989 system. In this respect, the Commission considers that the setting up of a Contract Committee, which is foreseen in the new Directive now entering the conciliation phase of the co‐decision procedure, is an important improvement on the 1989 system.

***

Question No 110 by Antoinette Spaak (H−0065/97)

Subject: Right to vote in municipal elections – failure of the Kingdom of Belgium to comply

The Member States were to bring into force the laws, regulations and administrative provisions to implement the requirements of Directive 94/80/EC(20) before 1 January 1996.

Although that date is now long past, the Kingdom of Belgium has still not done so, blatantly flouting European law. The Commission stated in its answer to my question H−0942/96(21) that it had initiated the procedure provided for in Article 169 of the EC Treaty.

When was the procedure initiated? What deadline did the Commission set for the Kingdom of Belgium in its reasoned opinion? Will the Commission take account of the constitutional reform needed for the Kingdom of Belgium to comply with its obligations and the length of the procedure when assessing the advisability of bringing an action before the Court of Justice?

Answer

As the Honourable Member correctly points out, Council Directive 94/80/EC of 19 December 1994, based on Article 8 B (2) of the EC Treaty, determined the procedures for exercising the right to vote in municipal elections for Union citizens residing in a Member State of which they are not nationals.

Member States had until 1 January 1996 to implement the laws, regulations and administrative provisions in order to comply with this directive and thus give Union citizens residing in their country the right to vote in municipal elections.

In the absence of any information concerning national implementing measures on the part of Belgium, the Commission initiated the procedure laid down by Article 169 of the EC Treaty. This procedure is at present at the reasoned opinion stage and Belgium has yet to react to it.

It is now up to the Commission to decide whether or not to bring the matter to the Court of Justice, while taking into account the need for Belgium to undertake constitutional reform in order to honour its obligations, a reform which the entry into force of the Treaty on European Union on 1 November 1993 has rendered inevitable.

Question No 111 by Jyrki Otila (H−0066/97)

Subject: Abolition of tax on sweets

According to information in the press, the Commission proposes to take action against the tax on sweets in Finland and the system for its collection pursuant to Article 169 of the Treaty of Rome. The reason for this action is Finland's unequal taxation practice, which has targeted traditional sweeteners to the benefit of xylitol.

With the opening up of the Internal Market in 1993 and Finland's accession to the EU in 1995, the Finnish and European confectionery industry suffered exceptional restrictions on trade in the EU as a result of the confectionery tax payable in Finland. Although, in the Internal Market, border formalities should have disappeared altogether, the product tax on confectionery proved impossible to collect in practice without some kind of control at Finland's borders. This is an unnecessary obstacle to trade for Finnish and European confectionery manufacturers, particularly since all the other EU countries with the exception of Denmark have abolished their confectionery taxes.

Does the Commission agree that the confectionery tax causes problems with trade on the Internal Market and does it propose to take measures, in accordance with the powers accorded to it under Article 99 of the Treaty of Rome, to abolish completely the confectionery tax in Finland?

Answer

Current Community excise legislation only allows Member States to retain national excise duties on mineral oils, alcoholic beverages and manufactured tobacco products and only on condition that the introduction or retention of these national duties does not give rise, in trade between Member States, to controls when crossing a border.

Following question 3657/95 by Mr Toivonen, the Commission undertook an evaluation of the procedures for collecting the tax on confectionery as applied in Finland. In order to allow Finland to provide further details on this matter, it was sent an administrative letter at the end of 1996.

Given the present situation, the Commission does not consider it appropriate to submit harmonization proposals on the subject of taxes on confectionery.

Question No 112 by Richard Howitt (H−0069/97)

Subject: Windfall tax

What consideration or discussions took place in or with the Commission concerning the imposition of a windfall tax on banks in the United Kingdom by the UK Government in 1981, and its compatibility with the then European Community law? Can the Commission identify the relevant legislation and confirm that none passed by 1981 or subsequently could prevent the use of a windfall tax by any Member State, as long as it does not discriminate unfairly between companies?

Answer

In the United Kingdom, Section 134 and Schedule 17 of the Finance Act 1981 introduced a one‐off windfall levy on banks. This levy was calculated by reference to the amount of non‐interest bearing deposits held by banks over a given period. The levy did not give rise to any consideration or discussions in or with the Commission concerning its compatibility with EC law.

As to the second part of the question, it is not possible for the Commission to take a view on the compatibility of tax legislation with Community law without seeing the particular legislation in question.

Question No 113 by Bernie Malone (H−0078/97)

Subject: Dublin port tunnel

Is the Commission aware that the project to build a tunnel linking Dublin Port with the national motorway network is being delayed due to concerns about structural damage to houses if the ‚new Austrian tunnelling method’ is used and the probable increase in pollution from exhaust emissions at the Northern Portal?

Will the Commission confirm that no funding will be provided for the tunnel's construction until the Irish Government submits an application and the Commission is satisfied, on the basis of the information supplied, that there will be no harmful effect on the environment, especially in relation to the two concerns mentioned above?

Will the project receive the full 75 % funding from the Cohesion Fund once contracts are entered into before 31 December 1999, even if the work is not finished until after 31 December 2000?

Answer

The European Commission's financial participation in the Dublin port access project to date has been limited to a grant of 5.8 MECU from the Cohesion fund for part of the planning and design work for the project. The assistance provided does not cover any element of land acquisition or construction. The intention of providing this aid is to allow the authorities to thoroughly investigate all economic and environment aspects of the proposed project.

The granting of assistance from the Cohesion Fund for one phase of project does not legally commit the European Commisssion to grant aiding any subsequent phase of a project. Therefore, a further separate application for assistance will have to be submitted before funding for the construction phase of the project can be granted. Any such application will be judged on the merits of the economic and environment arguments advanced by the Irish authorities. The agreement of the Commission will be required before a decision is taken to grant assistance.

The Commission understands that a decision has been taken in principle to toll the Dublin port access route. Assuming that this is the case and assuming that any application for Cohesion Fund assistance is acceptable on economic and environmental grounds, the commission's practice in such cases would be to grant aid the cost less the revenue generated by tolling. In these circumstances, it would be unlikely that the rate of assistance would be the maximum possible (85 %). In the absence of an application for assistance and the information required to determine the cost of the project and the revenue likely to be generated, it is impossible to say at this stage what the aid rate would be.

Under the Community's budgetary system, Cohesion Fund assistance which is committed to a project from the budget of one year may be drawn down in the form of payments to the beneficiary member state in subsequent years. Other than an initial advance, periodic payments related to the project would be made only on condition that the member state demonstrates that the financial and physical progress of the project is proceeding satisfactorily towards completion.

Under the current financial perspectives, commitment appropriations for the
Cohesion Fund are available only until the end of 1999. There are at present a large number of competing demands for the limited amount of Cohesion Fund assistance for Ireland which remains uncommitted.

***

Question No 114 by Karin Riis‐jørgensen (H−0081/97)

Subject: Sale of Danish electricity company shares to foreign undertakins

The internal market rules on the purchase and sale of shares across borders also, according to the EU Treaty, applies to the electricity industry. The Danish Government has just tabled draft legislation which includes rules for the sale of shares by electricity companies.

The remarks appended to the bill include the words 'the aim of the legislation is to reduce the special incentive to local authorities to sell electricity supply holdings to companies not covered by the price rules laid down in the electricity supply act'.

Does not the Commission consider that this remark seeks to discriminate against foreign undertakings in their ability to buy shares in Danish electricity companies if the legislation is adopted by the Danish Folketing?

Answer

The Commission is not aware of any specific provisions within the provisions of the proposed legislation which would discriminate against the participation of Community undertakings in the share capital of Danish electricity companies. Were such a restriction to exist in the draft legal text and be subsequently adopted, the question of its compatibility with EC Treaty provisions on establishment (Art. 52 ff) and capital movements (Article 73b ff) would be examined, in the context of the exceptions to the freedoms in the relevant articles. On the basis of the results of this examination, were the Commission to consider that the measure was not in accordance with the Treaty provisions, appropriate action would be taken.

***

Question No 115 by Gerard Collins (H−0091/97)

Subject: Objective 1 regions

When does the Commission intend to table its initial proposals listing the regions (or parts of regions) which it considers should continue to qualify for Objective 1 status, thereby qualifying for maximum Structural Fund grant aid levels, after 1999?

Answer

The list of less‐developed regions eligible for assistance under Objective 1 of the Structural Funds was drawn up by the Council in July 1993 on the basis of a Commission proposal. That list will remain valid until the end of 1999.

As regards the period after 1999, the Commission will propose revised regulations for the Structural Funds and a new list of less‐developed regions in the 15 Member States as soon as possible after the European Council has laid down the financial perspective for that period.

The financial perspective and guidelines for the future cohesion policy will be based on the relevant proposals (SANTER package) which the Commission intends to adopt as soon as the Intergovernmental Conference has ended.

***

Question No 116 by James Fitzsimons (H−0092/97)

Subject: Severe effect of soaring European paper prices on large users of paper in Ireland

Is the Commission aware that European wood pulp manufacturers and suppliers of raw paper have increased their prices significantly in recent months, and that the price of carbonless paper, copier paper and woodfree offset paper have gone up by as much as 20 % in the last three months, all of which is having a severe effect on large users of paper in Ireland such as manufacturers, printing companies and SMEs, and will the Commission, as a matter of urgency, re‐investigate possible paper price fixing among European paper manufacturers and will it outline what measures it will take to halt this rise?

Answer

The Commission is closely following price developments and the competitive situation in the paper and paper pulp sector.

The prices of products in this sector rise and fall constantly in response to a wide variety of factors, such as shortterm internal Community demand and the pressure exerted by external demand.

Thus, in 1996, certain sources report that paper pulp producers were forced to abandon a number of moves to raise prices because of a substantial drop in internal consumption (almost 3 %).

However, in this connection, the Commission is closely monitoring the possibility of producers employing concerted practices which might constitute breaches of the relevant provisions of Community law.

This is why the Commission is actively pursuing its investigations in the newsprint sector, although it is not possible at this stage to forecast precisely when a decision may be taken.

However, the Commission has noted the price increases for the categories of paper referred to by the Honourable Member (carbonless paper, copier paper and wood‐free offset paper), but as yet it has received no complaints concerning the employment of concerted practices among the producers concerned with a view to fixing or maintaining prices at an artificially high level.

The Commission will certainly examine very closely any information it receives which points to a breach of the competition rules laid down by the Treaty in these various sectors.

***

Question No 117 by Liam Hyland (H−0093/97)

Subject: Decrease in Irish projects under the EU's LIFE programme for environmental projects

Can the Commission explain why in 1996 only one project from Ireland secured funding from the EU's LIFE programme for environmental projects and why in 1997 only two Irish projects are to be co‐financed from this programme?

Answer

Each year, LIFE proposals are evaluated on merit three times: by panels of independent evaluators, by the Member state administrations and the Commission. Only the best succeed to become full projects with Community co‐funding. The linkage between Member states and success rate is not assured. The relationship between number of proposals and success is more consistent and statistically credible – the higher the number of proposals, the higher probability of good proposals.

For 1995 (under LIFE I) less than 20 proposals were submitted to the Irish Authority for transmission to the Commission. Only one was judged to be good enough (start date 1996).

For 1996 (under the new LIFE II) only nine proposals were submitted. Two were judged on merit to be good enough. At the beginning of February 1997 neither has been able to signal acceptance of the conditions.

***

Question No 118 by Peter Sichrovsky (H−0105/97)

Subject: Year against Racism – special programmes for young people

The European Union has devoted this year to the fight against racism. Violence motivated by racism and xenophobia is steadily growing, especially among young people.

What practical educational programmes and activities will the Commission be launching with a view to preventing or eliminating racial prejudices among young people by means of information campaigns and intercultural events? What financial support will be provided for these activities?

Young people unemployed and uncertain about the purpose of life are focusing their prejudices on religious as well as ethnic minorities.

How is the Commission planning to improve young people's understanding of religious minorities as part of an anti‐racism campaign in the widest sense?

With the prospect of the EU's eastward enlargement, future Member States should already be involved in a campaign against racism, extremism and xenophobia among young people.

To what extent are there plans for cooperation with former Eastern Bloc countries in the fight against racism (in the widest sense)?

Answer

The European Year against Racism will be an exercise in partnership and communication: the Commission aims to involve all possible actors in the struggle against racism, xenophobia and anti‐semitism.

The Year will try to mobilise societies as a whole; there will be no European level information campaigns or intercultural events specifically aimed at young people.

Discrimination against religious minorities, such as anti‐semitism, is encompassed in the general framework of the Year and the subject will be dealt with appropriately.

The limitations both in time and in budget made it impossible to enlarge the European Year against Racism to states which have applied for membership. However, co‐operation with the Council of Europe has been established and the Year aims to build on the experiences of the Council of Europe's Youth Campaign against Racism.

Member States might have special events or campaigns aimed at the young and financial support at national level depends on the national budgets available for the Year.

***

Question No 119 by Anne Mcintosh (H−0855/96)

Subject: Measures to prevent coastal erosion in the Naze area of North Essex

What measures does the Commission propose to take, with particular regard to the problems such as those experienced in the Naze area of Essex, to protect our coastlines from damaging erosion and facilitate proper integrated management of such coastal zones?

Could the Commission explain further the objectives of a European spatial development programme and detail the ways in which it is hoped these can be accomplished?

Could the Commisison detail the number and location of the projects that have been set up under the three year demonstration programme, further to its information document of February 1996, and the reasons for their choice?

Answer

The Commission does not intend to propose specific measures concerning coastal erosion in the Naze area of Essex.

The aim of the European Spatial Development Perspective Programmes is, on the basis of a coherent vision of the main strengths, weaknesses, opportunities and threats characterising the European territory, to set up an integrated, multi‐sectoral and indicative strategy for its development. This strategy is to ensure consistency and complementarity between the Member State's territorial policies and to co‐ordinate the territorial aspects of the Community policies.

As with any strategic document, the ESDP is not, however, merely intended to set out a number of objectives. It is also a tool for identifying the measures and actions likely to contribute to these objectives.

With the adoption of a third strand of INTERREG II on transnational co‐operation in the field of spatial planning, the Community has for the first time the opportunity to demonstrate how the principles and objectives of the ESDP can be translated into practical co‐operation programmes as well as the benefits, notably in terms of cost‐efficiency in the longer run, to be derived from applying a spatial planning approach. Seven such Programmes concerning respectively the Baltic Sea Region, the North Sea, the North Western Metropolitan Area, the Atlantic Space, South Eastern Europe and the 'Adrianube' (Central Europe, Adriatic, Danube) are expected to be submitted to the Commission by March the 10th 1997.

Moreover, five pilot actions under article 10 of the ERDF, also geared to spatial planning have already been announced and will reach the Commission by the same date. These pilot‐actions are targeting respectively the Northern periphery, the Eastern Alps, the South Eastern Mediterranean, the Rhine‐Lotharingia area and Mediterranean Doors (Portugal, Spain and Morocco).

A first draft of the European Spatial Development Perspective will be presented to the Ministers responsible for Spatial Planning meeting in Noordwijk early June. The Perspective will be subject to a wide public debate to take place in the second half of this year.

Twenty‐six project areas have been retained so far to constitute the basis for the demonstration programme on integrated management of coastal zones. Twelve projects have been selected under the LIFE Programme. Four networks, composed of a total of twelve areas have been pre‐selected under the TERRA Programme. Provided the negotiations regarding the contents and the eligibility of the projects have been completed successfully the final selection of the projects is expected to take place before the summer of 1997. Two projects funded under the PHARE programme have expressed interest to participate in the demonstration programme. Details of the project areas are freely available but are too long to be presented here.

The choice of the projects was based on the criteria announced in the tender documentation of the LiFE Instrument (XI/660/94) and the TERRA Programme.

***

Question No 120 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0997/96)

Subject: Failure to announce a competition for the project for the completion of the Athens Music Centre

On 12 July 1996 building licence No. 702 was granted for the completion of the Athens Music Centre. However, on 18 October 1996 the Panhellenic Association of Architects lodged an appeal to have this licence invalidated, and a decision is still pending. This project which includes an underground garage and an opera or cultural or conference centre has an overall budget in excess of Drs. 10 billion which will be paid for entirely out of the public purse and Community funds.

Given that, for projects with a budget in excess of Drs. 1.5 billion (ECU 5 million) undertaken with public monies, a competition must be announced and published in accordance with the provisions of Directive 93/37/EEC(22) , will the Commission say whether the mandatory competition for the execution of the project has been announced and published and, if not, what measures it intends to take?

Answer

The Commission informs the Honourable Member that the announcement of a competition for the completion of the Athens Music Centre was published in the Official Journal of the European Communities of 8 January 1997.(23)
